The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Definition and Types of Syllogism
Defines syllogism and its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conjunctive and conditional syllogisms and identify the defining characteristic of a syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a set of propositions that necessarily imply another by their very essence.

This passage opens the chapter on qiyas, the central tool of logical inference. It defines what a syllogism is and immediately divides it into two fundamental types. Understanding this division is crucial for correctly applying syllogistic reasoning in Islamic sciences, where arguments must be properly structured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the line that defines qiyas. What are the two essential components mentioned? The definition states that a syllogism is formed from propositions (qadaya) that necessarily imply (mustalzima) another statement by their essence (bi al-dhat).
2
Extract the two types
Find where the text divides syllogism into two types. What are their names? The passage says: 'thumma al-qiyasu 'indahum qismani' and then names them: iqtirani (conjunctive) and istithna'i (conditional).
3
Distinguish the types
According to the passage, what is the key feature of the conjunctive (iqtirani) syllogism? The text says it 'indicates the conclusion by its power and is specific to categorical propositions' (dalla 'ala al-natijati bi quwwatin wa khtassa bi al-hamliyya). The conditional type is not described here but is implied as the other division.
4
Connect to Islamic science
Think of how a conditional syllogism might be used in a theological argument. For example: 'If the world is temporal, then it has a creator. The world is temporal. Therefore, it has a creator.' This is a conditional syllogism (istithna'i). The conjunctive type uses categorical premises like 'Every human is mortal.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(بَابٌ فِي القِيَاسِ)
(۷۳) إِنَّ القِيَاسَ» مِنْ قَضَايَا صُوِّرَا مُسْتَلْزِمَا بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَا ( ٧٤) ثُمَّ القِيَاسُ عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ : فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِـ: «الاِقْتِرَانِي» (٧٥) وَهْوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَتِيجَةِ بِقُوَّةٍ وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ (٧٦) فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ فَرَكَّبَا مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا (۷۷) وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَانْظُرَا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ مُخْتَبِرَا (۷۸) فَإِنَّ لَازِمَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ بِحَسَبِ المُقَدِّمَـاتِ آتِ (۷۹) وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى) فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُهَا فِي الكُبْرَى» (۸۰) وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرٍ : «صُغْرَاهُمَا » وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرٍ: «كُبْرَاهُمَا» (۸۱) وَأَصْغَرٌ» فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ وَوَسَطُ يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنْتَاجِ
Chapter on Syllogism. (73) Indeed, the syllogism is formed from propositions, necessarily implying by essence another statement. (74) Then the syllogism according to them is two types: from it there is what is called 'conjunctive' (iqtirani) (75) and it is that which indicates the conclusion by its power and is specific to categorical propositions. (76) If you intend its composition, then compose its premises according to what is necessary. (77) And arrange the premises and look at its sound from the corrupt, testing. (78) For the necessary consequent of the premises is coming according to the premises. (79) And what among the premises is the minor? It is necessary that it be subsumed under the major. (80) And that which has the minor term is 'its minor', and that which has the major term is 'its major'. (81) And the minor is that which is subsumed, and the middle is omitted in the production.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on qiyas. First, it defines syllogism: a set of propositions that, by their very nature (bi-al-dhat), entail another proposition (the conclusion). Then it divides syllogism into two types: iqtirani (conjunctive) and istithna'i (conditional). The iqtirani is the one that yields the conclusion by its own strength and works with categorical (hamli) propositions. The passage then gives instructions for composing a syllogism: arrange the premises correctly and test them; the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises. It then identifies the minor premise (sughra) as the one that contains the minor term (the subject of the conclusion) and the major premise (kubra) as the one with the major term (the predicate of the conclusion). The minor term is that which is subsumed under the major term. The middle term (wasat) is the one that is omitted (mulgha) in the conclusion; it appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. For example, in 'Every human is mortal. Every rational is human. Therefore, every rational is mortal.' The middle term is 'human'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a syllogism?
A) It is composed of two propositions.
B) It necessarily implies another proposition by its essence.
C) It uses only categorical propositions.
D) It always has three terms.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is defined by necessary implication; it is either conjunctive (categorical) or conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a simple conditional syllogism using an Islamic example (e.g., 'If Allah is just, then the righteous are rewarded. Allah is just. Therefore, the righteous are rewarded.'). Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the exception (istithna).

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Structure of a Conjunctive Syllogism
Explains the structure of a conjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor premise, major premise, minor term, major term, and middle term in a conjunctive syllogism.

Core Idea
The middle term is the key that connects the premises and disappears in the conclusion.

The passage now gives practical instructions for composing a conjunctive syllogism. It defines the roles of the minor and major premises and introduces the crucial concept of the middle term, which is omitted in the conclusion. This structure is the backbone of categorical logic used in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the instructions for composition
Find the lines that tell you how to compose a syllogism (lines 76-81). The text gives directives: 'If you intend its composition, then compose its premises according to what is necessary. And arrange the premises and look at its sound from the corrupt, testing.'
2
Identify the minor premise (sughra)
The text says: 'And what among the premises is the minor? It is necessary that it be subsumed under the major.' The minor premise contains the minor term (the subject of the conclusion) and is subsumed under the major premise.
In a categorical syllogism, the minor premise is the one that contains the subject of the conclusion.
3
Identify the major premise (kubra)
The text says: 'And that which has the major term is its major.' The major premise contains the major term (the predicate of the conclusion).
4
Recognize the three terms
The passage mentions three terms: the minor term (hadd asghar), which is subsumed; the major term (hadd akbar), which does the subsuming; and the middle term (wasat), which is omitted (mulgha) in the conclusion. The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
For example, in 'Every human is mortal. Every rational is human. Therefore, every rational is mortal.' The minor term is 'rational', the major term is 'mortal', and the middle term is 'human'.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Every nabidh is intoxicant. Therefore, every nabidh is haram.' Identify the minor premise (Every nabidh is intoxicant), major premise (Every intoxicant is haram), minor term (nabidh), major term (haram), and middle term (intoxicant).

The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
The Four Figures of the Syllogism
Defines figure and lists the first figure based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is all about where the middle term sits.

In this lesson, we unpack what logicians mean by 'shakl' (figure) of a categorical syllogism. The figure depends solely on the position of the middle term in the two premises: whether it is subject or predicate. This is distinct from the 'darb' (mood) which considers quantifiers and quality. Understanding the figures is essential for assessing validity in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms of the syllogism
In any categorical syllogism, there are three terms: subject (major term), predicate (minor term), and middle term. The middle term is the one that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
2
Locate the middle term in the minor premise
Check whether the middle term is the subject or the predicate in the first premise (al-muqaddima al-sughra).
3
Locate the middle term in the major premise
Check whether the middle term is the subject or the predicate in the second premise (al-muqaddima al-kubra).
4
Determine the figure using the four possibilities
First figure: predicate in minor, subject in major. Second figure: predicate in both. Third figure: subject in both. Fourth figure: subject in minor, predicate in major.
The source passage explicitly mentions only the first figure, but the full set of four is standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فَصْلٌ فِي الأَشْكَالِ)
(۸۲) «الشَّكْلُ عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ النَّاسِ يُطْلَقُ عَنْ قَضِيَّتَيْ قِيَاسِ (۸۳) مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ إِذْ ذَاكَ بِالضَّرْبِ لَهُ يُشَارُ (٨٤) وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَشْكَالٌ فَقَط أَرْبَعَةٌ بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَط : (٨٥) حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى وَضْعُهُ بِكُبْرَى يُدْعَى بِ: «شَكْلِ أَوَّلِ وَيُدْرَى
Chapter on Figures. (82) The figure according to these people is applied to the two propositions of the syllogism (83) without considering the quantifiers, as then it is indicated by the mood. (84) And for the premises, there are only four figures according to the middle term: (85) It [the middle term] is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, called 'first figure', and it is known.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of 'shakl' (figure) of a syllogism. The figure concerns only the two premises (minor and major) without regard to quality or quantity (the quantifiers), which are later considered in the 'darb' (mood). There are exactly four figures based on the position of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in the two premises. The first figure occurs when the middle term is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise. For example, in 'Every human is mortal. Every rational is human. Therefore, every rational is mortal.' The middle 'human' is predicate in minor, subject in major. The other figures will have different arrangements (middle as subject in both, predicate in both, or subject in minor and predicate in major). The text only gives the first figure here, but by implication there are three others.

Key Terms
شكل
ضرب
حد وسط
حمل
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Middle is subject in both premises
B) Middle is predicate in both premises
C) Middle is predicate in minor and subject in major
D) Middle is subject in minor and predicate in major

Key Takeaway
The figure of a syllogism is determined exclusively by the position of the middle term in the two premises, and there are four figures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism
beginner
8 min
Definition and Division of Syllogism (Qiyas)
Defines syllogism and its two types with term components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of syllogism and distinguish the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a speech that forces a conclusion from premises; its two types are iqtirani and istithna'i.

The Aristotelian syllogism, as adopted by Islamic logicians, is the backbone of demonstrative reasoning. This lesson breaks down the definition of qiyas and its primary division, focusing on the conjunctive (iqtirani) type restricted to categorical propositions. You will learn the roles of the minor, major, and middle terms that form its structure.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the definition of syllogism
The passage defines qiyas as 'speech composed of premises that entail another statement by itself.' Identify the two essential components: the premises (muqaddimat) and the conclusion (natija). The entailment is intrinsic, not dependent on external factors.
This definition excludes mere rhetorical speech or incomplete arguments.
2
Distinguish the two types of syllogism
The syllogism is divided into iqtirani (conjunctive) and istithna'i (exceptive). Iqtirani indicates the conclusion 'by potentiality' (bil-quwwa) and uses only categorical (hamli) premises. Istithna'i will be covered later.
Iqtirani is also called 'simple syllogism' and is the primary type studied.
3
Identify the three terms in a categorical syllogism
In any categorical syllogism, there are three terms: the minor term (subject of the conclusion), the major term (predicate of the conclusion), and the middle term (which appears in both premises but is eliminated in the conclusion). The minor premise contains the minor term and the middle; the major premise contains the major term and the middle.
The passage says: 'the minor term is what is included, and the middle term is eliminated in production.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القياس) إن القياس من قضايا صورا مستلزما بالذات قولا آخرا ثم القياس عندهم قسمان: فمنه ما يدعى بـ: «الاقتراني» وهو الذي دل على النتيجة بقوة واختص بالحملية فإن ترد تركيبه فركبا مقدماته على ما وجبا ورتب المقدمات وانظرا صحيحها من فاسد مختبرا فإن لازم المقدمات بحسب المقدمات آت وما من المقدمات صغرى) فيجب اندراجها في الكبرى» وذات حد أصغر: «صغراهما » وذات حد أكبر: «كبراهما» وأصغر» فذاك ذو اندراج ووسط يلغى لدى الإنتاج
Chapter on Syllogism. Indeed the syllogism is composed of premises structured so as to entail by itself another statement. Then the syllogism among them is of two types: among them is that which is called 'conjunctive' (iqtirani), and it is that which indicates the conclusion by potentiality and is restricted to categoricals. If you want to compose it, then compose its premises as required. Arrange the premises and examine the sound from the corrupt, testing. For the necessary consequent of the premises comes in accordance with the premises. And what from the premises is the minor, so its inclusion in the major is necessary. And that which has the minor term is its minor premise, and that which has the major term is its major premise. And the minor term is what is included, and the middle term is eliminated in production.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines syllogism (qiyas) as a composition of premises that necessarily produce a conclusion. The author then divides syllogism into two types: conjunctive (iqtirani), which works by potentiality (i.e., the conclusion is not explicitly stated but follows from the premises), and exceptive (istithna'i), which will be discussed later. The conjunctive is restricted to categorical (hamli) propositions. The text instructs one to arrange the premises in order (minor then major? actually the order is important for validity). It emphasizes that the necessary result of the premises depends on the premises themselves. Then it explains the terms: the minor premise is that which contains the minor term (subject of the conclusion), the major premise contains the major term (predicate), and the middle term is the term that is repeated but eliminated in the conclusion. The minor term must be included under the major, i.e., the middle term establishes that inclusion.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): A syllogism: a speech composed of premises that by themselves necessitate another statement (the conclusion). [Note: In later texts, qiyas can also refer to analogical reasoning in fiqh, but here it denotes categorical syllogism.]
الاقتراني (al-iqtirani): Conjunctive syllogism: a syllogism that implies the conclusion by potentiality (bil-quwwa) not explicitly, and is composed of categorical premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of syllogism is restricted to categorical propositions (hamiliyyāt) and indicates the conclusion by potentiality (bi'l-quwwa)?
A) al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī
B) al-qiyās al-iqṭirānī
C) al-qiyās al-ḥamlī
D) al-qiyās al-sharṭī

Key Takeaway
A syllogism (qiyas) is a speech that entails a conclusion; its iqtirani type is based on categorical propositions and contains a middle term that connects the minor and major terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is haram.' Identify the minor term, major term, and middle term, then determine which premise is the minor and which is the major.
?
Classify the following as iqtirani or istithna'i: 'If it rains then the ground is wet; it is raining; therefore the ground is wet.' Explain why it fits or does not fit the iqtirani type described in the passage.

The Syllogism

5 min
What is Figure (Shakl)?
Defines figure and enumerates the four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'figure' (shakl) in categorical syllogisms and distinguish it from mood (darb).

Core Idea
Figure is arrangement of premises; quantifiers determine mood, not figure.

In syllogistic logic, the figure of a syllogism refers to the position of the middle term in the two premises. This lesson explains what figure means and why quantifiers are irrelevant to it, setting the stage for the four figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the sentence that defines 'shakl' in the Arabic passage. It begins with 'الشكل عند هؤلاء الناس يطلق عن قضيتي قياس...'. Note the key elements: 'arrangement of the two premises' and 'without considering the quantifiers'.
The definition explicitly excludes quantifiers because they belong to mood (darb), not figure.
2
Understand the exclusion of quantifiers
Why does the author say 'من غير أن تعتبر الأسوار'? Because quantifiers (all, some, no, not all) determine the mood, which is a separate property. Figure is purely about the position of the middle term.
3
Contrast figure and mood
Figure is about arrangement of terms; mood is about the types of propositions (universal affirmative, etc.). This distinction is crucial for analyzing syllogisms correctly.
For example, a Barbara syllogism (universal affirmative premises) can appear in the first figure only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأشكال) «الشكل عند هؤلاء الناس يطلق عن قضيتي قياس من غير أن تعتبر الأسوار إذ ذاك بالضرب له يشار وللمقدمات أشكال فقط أربعة بحسب الحد الوسط : حمل بصغرى وضعه بكبرى يدعى بـ: «شكل أول» ويدرى وحمله في الكل: «ثانيا» عرف ووضعه في الكل: «ثالثا» ألف ورابع الأشكال : عكس الأول وهي على الترتيب في التكمل فحيث عن هذا النظام يعدل ففاسد النظام
Chapter on Figures. 'The figure among these people is applied to the two premises of a syllogism without considering the quantifiers, because then the mode is indicated for it. The premises have only four figures according to the middle term: (1) Its predicate in the minor and its subject in the major is called 'first figure' and is known; (2) Its predicate in both is called 'second' and is known; (3) Its subject in both is called 'third' and is composed; (4) And the fourth figure is the converse of the first. They are in order of perfection. So wherever this order is deviated, it is a corrupt system.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines figure (shakl) as the arrangement of the two premises of a syllogism with respect to the middle term, ignoring the quantifiers (sawar) since those determine the mood (darb) not the figure. There are exactly four figures based on the position of the middle term. The first figure: the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major. The second figure: the middle term is predicate in both premises. The third figure: the middle term is subject in both premises. The fourth figure: the middle term is the converse of the first (i.e., subject in minor and predicate in major). These are arranged in order of perfection (the first being most perfect, then second, etc.). Any deviation from this natural order produces an invalid figure.

Key Terms

الحد الوسط

The Four Figures of the Syllogism
Defines figure and enumerates the four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and describe the four figures based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
Four figures exist; the first is most perfect, and the fourth is the converse of the first.

The passage enumerates exactly four figures based on the middle term's position: predicate in minor and subject in major (1st), predicate in both (2nd), subject in both (3rd), and its converse (4th). They are arranged in order of perfection. This lesson breaks down each figure for memorization and application.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the four arrangements
For each figure, note where the middle term appears in the minor and major premises. Use the descriptions in the passage: first (predicate in minor, subject in major), second (predicate in both), third (subject in both), fourth (subject in minor, predicate in major).
The fourth figure is the converse of the first—swap the positions of the middle term.
2
Understand the order of perfection
The passage says the figures are 'on the order of perfection' (على الترتيب في التكمل). The first figure is most perfect because it is the most natural and directly yields conclusions without conversion. The other figures require conversion or indirect reasoning.
Any deviation from this order produces a corrupt system (فاسد النظام) meaning an invalid syllogism.
3
Practice identifying figures
Given a syllogism, determine which figure it belongs to by locating the middle term in the two premises. For example: 'All A are B, No C is B, so No C is A.' The middle term B is predicate in both premises, so it is the second figure.

The Syllogism

Productivity Conditions for Each Syllogistic Figure
States the necessary conditions for valid syllogisms in each figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given categorical syllogism satisfies the productivity conditions for its figure.

Core Idea
Each figure has precise conditions; only a few moods are productive.

In this passage, the author lays out the essential conditions that a categorical syllogism must meet for each figure to yield a valid conclusion. These conditions are the logical 'filters' that separate productive moods from sterile ones. Understanding them is crucial for evaluating any syllogism in the Islamic logical tradition, especially in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure of the syllogism
Determine the position of the middle term. In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. In the second, it is predicate of both. In the third, subject of both. In the fourth, it is predicate of the major and subject of the minor.
2
Check the first-figure conditions
The minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. If either condition fails, the syllogism is not productive.
Example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (major universal). Nabidh is an intoxicant (minor affirmative). Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' This is valid.
3
Check the second-figure conditions
The two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. If the major is not universal, the syllogism is not productive.
Example: 'No injustice is good (major universal negative). Some deeds are good (minor affirmative). Therefore some deeds are not injustice.' This is valid.
4
Check the third and fourth figures
For the third figure, both premises must be affirmative and at least one must be universal. For the fourth figure, avoid combining two 'inferiorities' (particular negatives) except the one allowed mood: minor affirmative particular and major negative universal.
Example of valid third figure: 'Some acts of worship are obligatory (minor particular affirmative). All obligatory acts are rewarded (major universal affirmative). Therefore some rewarded acts are acts of worship.' This is a third-figure valid mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول فشرطه : الإيجاب في صغراه وإن ترى كلية كبراه والثان : أن يختلفا في الكيف مع كلية الكبرى له شرط وقع والثالث : الإيجاب في صغراهما وإن ترى كلية إحداهما ورابع : عدم جمع الخستين ؛ إلا بصورة ففيها يستبين صغراهما موجبة جزئيا كبراهما سالبة كلية
As for the first, its condition is: affirmation in its minor, and that you see its major as universal. The second: that they differ in quality with universality of the major; this condition holds. The third: affirmation in both its premises, and that you see one of them as universal. The fourth: avoidance of combining the two inferiorities, except in a form which clarifies: its minor is affirmative particular, its major is negative universal.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage gives the conditions for each figure to produce a valid conclusion (productive). For the first figure: the minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. For the second figure: the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and the major must be universal. For the third figure: both premises must be affirmative, and at least one must be universal. For the fourth figure: it must not combine the two inferiorities (i.e., be particular negative) except in one specific form: a minor affirmative particular and a major negative universal. This exception (the only productive mood in the fourth figure) is explicitly given as an example.

Key Terms

الشكل
الجزئية
الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Only certain combinations of premise qualities and quantities produce valid conclusions; these conditions are the rules of the syllogism.

Productive Moods by Figure
Lists the number of valid moods per figure and states general rules about conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recall and recite the number of productive moods for each of the four figures of the categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Only 19 specific moods produce a conclusion; the rest are unproductive.

In classical logic, not every combination of premise types yields a valid conclusion. The ulama of mantiq enumerated exactly how many productive moods each figure has. This passage provides those numbers as a memorization anchor, essential for quickly verifying the validity of any syllogistic argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four figures
Recall that syllogistic figures are defined by the position of the middle term. First figure: middle is subject in major and predicate in minor. Second: predicate in both. Third: subject in both. Fourth: predicate in major, subject in minor.
2
Memorize the numbers
First figure: 4 productive moods. Second figure: also 4. Third figure: 6. Fourth figure: 5. The line 'wa ghayru ma dhakartu lan yantija' means any other combination is unproductive.
Total = 4+4+6+5 = 19 productive moods.
3
Test the rule
Given a mood like 'AAA in the first figure', it is productive. But 'AAA in the second figure' is not a valid mood. Use this to check why some classical arguments fail.
This rule is a shortcut in usul al-fiqh for evaluating qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنتج لـ «أول» : أربعة، كـ «الثان»، ثم «ثالث»: فستة ورابع»: بخمسة قد أنتجا وغير ما ذكرته لن ينتجا وتتبع النتيجة الأخس من تلك المقدمات هكذا زكن وهذه الأشكال بالحمل مختصة وليس بالشرطي والحذف في بعض المقدمات أو النتيجة لعلم آت وتنتهي إلى ضرورة؛ لما من دور أو تسلسل قد لزما
So the productive moods for the first: four, like the second. Then the third: six. And the fourth: five have produced. And other than what I mentioned will not produce. And the conclusion follows the weaker of those premises; this is known. And these figures are restricted to categorical, not conditional. And deletion in some premises or the conclusion occurs due to knowledge coming later. And they end in necessity, because of whatever circularity or infinite regress has occurred.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage gives the number of valid moods for each figure: first figure has 4, second also 4, third has 6, fourth has 5. Any other combination is unproductive. Then it states a general rule: the conclusion follows the 'weaker' premise – meaning it takes the quality and quantity that are less strong (e.g., a negative outweighs affirmative, particular outweighs universal). The text then notes that these figures are only for categorical (hamli) syllogisms, not conditional (sharti). It also mentions that sometimes premises or conclusions may be omitted due to their being known (i.e., enthymemes). Finally, it asserts that all syllogisms reduce to necessary first-figure ones, avoiding circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul).

Key Terms
الناتج
الأخس

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 6
D) 7

Key Takeaway

The Rule of the Weaker Premise
Lists the number of valid moods per figure and states general rules about conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a categorical syllogism by applying the rule that the conclusion follows the weaker premise in quality and quantity.

Core Idea
The conclusion is always as weak as the weakest premise.

A crucial rule in categorical syllogisms: the conclusion inherits the weaker of the two premises. 'Weaker' means negative over affirmative and particular over universal. This passage states that rule explicitly, alongside other important notes about the scope of these figures and the necessity of reduction.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'weaker'
In syllogistic logic, a negative proposition is considered 'weaker' than an affirmative because it places a restriction. Similarly, a particular proposition is weaker than a universal because it covers a subset. The conclusion must take the weaker quality and quantity.
This is why a valid syllogism cannot have an affirmative conclusion if one premise is negative.
2
Apply to a concrete example
Take premises: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal affirmative) and 'Some nabidh is an intoxicant' (particular affirmative). The weaker is particular (since both affirmative). So conclusion must be particular affirmative: 'Some nabidh is forbidden.'
If one premise were negative, the conclusion would be negative.
3
Recognize the other rules
The passage also notes that these figures apply only to categorical syllogisms, not conditional. It mentions that premises can be omitted (enthymemes) and that all syllogisms ultimately reduce to a necessary first-figure form to avoid circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul).
This reduction is why logicians consider the first figure as the most perfect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنتج لـ «أول» : أربعة، كـ «الثان»، ثم «ثالث»: فستة ورابع»: بخمسة قد أنتجا وغير ما ذكرته لن ينتجا وتتبع النتيجة الأخس من تلك المقدمات هكذا زكن وهذه الأشكال بالحمل مختصة وليس بالشرطي والحذف في بعض المقدمات أو النتيجة لعلم آت وتنتهي إلى ضرورة؛ لما من دور أو تسلسل قد لزما
So the productive moods for the first: four, like the second. Then the third: six. And the fourth: five have produced. And other than what I mentioned will not produce. And the conclusion follows the weaker of those premises; this is known. And these figures are restricted to categorical, not conditional. And deletion in some premises or the conclusion occurs due to knowledge coming later. And they end in necessity, because of whatever circularity or infinite regress has occurred.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage gives the number of valid moods for each figure: first figure has 4, second also 4, third has 6, fourth has 5. Any other combination is unproductive. Then it states a general rule: the conclusion follows the 'weaker' premise – meaning it takes the quality and quantity that are less strong (e.g., a negative outweighs affirmative, particular outweighs universal). The text then notes that these figures are only for categorical (hamli) syllogisms, not conditional (sharti). It also mentions that sometimes premises or conclusions may be omitted due to their being known (i.e., enthymemes). Finally, it asserts that all syllogisms reduce to necessary first-figure ones, avoiding circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul).

Key Terms

The conclusion of a categorical syllogism must take the weaker quality and quantity of the premises.

What is an Exceptive Syllogism?
Defines exceptive syllogism and its actual conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish an exceptive syllogism from a conjunctive syllogism and identify its defining feature: it indicates the conclusion actually, not potentially.

Core Idea
The exceptive syllogism yields actual certainty, not mere potential.

This lesson introduces the exceptive syllogism (al-istithna'i), which is a conditional syllogism that directly produces a conclusion by affirming or denying one part of a conditional premise. Unlike the conjunctive syllogism that only prepares the ground, the exceptive gives the result immediately. Let's examine its definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Look for the term 'الاستثنائي' in the Arabic. It is also called 'الشرطي' (conditional) without doubt.
2
Understand the key phrase: 'بالفعل لا بالقوة'
The text says it indicates the conclusion actually (bil-fi'l) not potentially (bil-quwwa). This is the defining characteristic. The conjunctive syllogism only gives the conclusion potentially because it needs conversion or combination; the exceptive gives it directly.
3
Recognize the two types
The text then states 'فإن يك الشرطي ذا اتصال...' referring to conjunctive conditional, and 'وإن يكن منفصلا...' referring to disjunctive. This lesson focuses on the definition; the rules will follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي) ومنه ما يدعى بـ: الاستثنائي) يعرف بـ: «الشرطي بلا امتراء وهو : الذي دل على النتيجة أو ضدها بالفعل لا بالقوة فإن يك الشرطي ذا اتصال : أنتج وضع ذاك وضع التالي ورفع تال رفع أول ، ولا يلزم في عكسيهما لما انجلى وإن يكن منفصلا : فوضع ذا ينتج رفع ذاك، والعكس كذا وذاك في الأخص ، ثم إن يكن مانع جمع فبوضع ذا زكن رفع لذاك دون عكس، وإذا مانع رفع كان فهو عكس ذا
Chapter on the Exceptive. And from it is that which is called 'exceptive'; it is known as conditional without doubt. It is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not by potentiality. So if the conditional is conjunctive: affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent; its converse does not follow, as is clear. If it is disjunctive: then affirming one produces denying the other, and the converse is like that (i.e., denying one affirms the other) in the specific (i.e., when they are mutually exclusive in both cases). Then if it is a 'man' of conjunction (i.e., prevents combination only), then by affirming one, the denial of the other is known, without the converse. And if it is a 'man' of denial (i.e., prevents both absence), then the converse of that (i.e., affirming one denies the other? Actually: if it prevents both being false, then denying one affirms the other).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptive syllogism (istithna'i), which uses a conditional major premise and a categorical minor that affirms or denies one part. It is called 'conditional' (sharti) without doubt. Unlike the conjunctive (iqtirani), it yields the conclusion actually, not by potentiality. For the conjunctive conditional (muttasil, if-then), valid inferences are: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) yields affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent (modus tollens) yields denying the antecedent. The converse (affirming consequent or denying antecedent) does not follow. For the disjunctive conditional (munfasil, either-or), the rules depend on the type of disjunction. In a complete disjunction (man' al-jam' wa al-khuluww, i.e., exactly one true), affirming one yields denying the other, and denying one yields affirming the other. If it is only 'man' al-jam' (cannot both be true, but both may be false), then affirming one yields denying the other, but not vice versa. If it is only 'man' al-khuluww (cannot both be false, but both may be true), then denying one yields affirming the other, but not vice versa. The text covers these cases.

Key Terms
الاستثنائي
الشرطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the exceptive syllogism from the conjunctive?
A) It uses a conditional premise.
B) It indicates the conclusion actually, not potentially.
C) It uses a categorical premise.
D) It is always disjunctive.

Key Takeaway
The exceptive syllogism is a conditional syllogism that gives the conclusion directly, as opposed to the conjunctive which only potentially indicates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an exceptive syllogism on a theological topic of your choice, ensuring the conclusion follows actually.

Rules of the Conjunctive Conditional (Muttasil)
States valid inference rules for conjunctive conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply modus ponens and modus tollens in a conjunctive conditional syllogism and identify invalid forms.

Core Idea
In a conjunctive conditional, affirming the antecedent proves the consequent; denying the consequent proves denying the antecedent.

The passage now details the valid inference rules for the conjunctive conditional (if-then). It states that affirming the antecedent yields affirming the consequent (modus ponens), and denying the consequent yields denying the antecedent (modus tollens). The converse rules are invalid. Let's study these rules with Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type
The text says 'فإن يك الشرطي ذا اتصال' meaning if the conditional is conjunctive.
2
Extract the two valid rules
The text says: 'أنتج وضع ذاك وضع التالي ورفع تال رفع أول' meaning 'affirming that (antecedent) produces affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent.' These are modus ponens and modus tollens.
3
Recognize the invalid forms
The text adds 'ولا يلزم في عكسيهما' meaning 'the converse of each does not follow.' So affirming consequent and denying antecedent are invalid.
This is crucial to avoid logical errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي) ومنه ما يدعى بـ: الاستثنائي) يعرف بـ: «الشرطي بلا امتراء وهو : الذي دل على النتيجة أو ضدها بالفعل لا بالقوة فإن يك الشرطي ذا اتصال : أنتج وضع ذاك وضع التالي ورفع تال رفع أول ، ولا يلزم في عكسيهما لما انجلى وإن يكن منفصلا : فوضع ذا ينتج رفع ذاك، والعكس كذا وذاك في الأخص ، ثم إن يكن مانع جمع فبوضع ذا زكن رفع لذاك دون عكس، وإذا مانع رفع كان فهو عكس ذا
Chapter on the Exceptive. And from it is that which is called 'exceptive'; it is known as conditional without doubt. It is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not by potentiality. So if the conditional is conjunctive: affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent; its converse does not follow, as is clear. If it is disjunctive: then affirming one produces denying the other, and the converse is like that (i.e., denying one affirms the other) in the specific (i.e., when they are mutually exclusive in both cases). Then if it is a 'man' of conjunction (i.e., prevents combination only), then by affirming one, the denial of the other is known, without the converse. And if it is a 'man' of denial (i.e., prevents both absence), then the converse of that (i.e., affirming one denies the other? Actually: if it prevents both being false, then denying one affirms the other).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptive syllogism (istithna'i), which uses a conditional major premise and a categorical minor that affirms or denies one part. It is called 'conditional' (sharti) without doubt. Unlike the conjunctive (iqtirani), it yields the conclusion actually, not by potentiality. For the conjunctive conditional (muttasil, if-then), valid inferences are: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) yields affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent (modus tollens) yields denying the antecedent. The converse (affirming consequent or denying antecedent) does not follow. For the disjunctive conditional (munfasil, either-or), the rules depend on the type of disjunction. In a complete disjunction (man' al-jam' wa al-khuluww, i.e., exactly one true), affirming one yields denying the other, and denying one yields affirming the other. If it is only 'man' al-jam' (cannot both be true, but both may be false), then affirming one yields denying the other, but not vice versa. If it is only 'man' al-khuluww (cannot both be false, but both may be true), then denying one yields affirming the other, but not vice versa. The text covers these cases.

Key Terms
الشرطي المتصل
المقدم
التالي

In a conjunctive conditional, only two inferences are valid: modus ponens and modus tollens; the converses are invalid.

Rules of the Disjunctive Conditional (Munfasil)
Details inference rules for disjunctive conditional based on type of disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunction (man' al-jam', man' al-khuluww, both) and apply the correct inference rules for each.

Core Idea
Disjunctive syllogism rules depend on whether the disjunction excludes both being true, both being false, or both.

The passage explains that for a disjunctive conditional (either-or), the inference rules vary based on the type of disjunction. It mentions three cases: when it prevents combination only (man' al-jam'), when it prevents both being false (man' al-khuluww), and when it prevents both (man' al-jam' wa al-khuluww). Let's break down each case with Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive type
The text says 'وإن يكن منفصلا' then lists conditions: 'فإن يكن مانع جمع' (if it prevents combination), 'وإذا مانع رفع كان' (if it prevents both being false). The complete disjunction prevents both.
2
Understand the rule for man' al-jam'
The text says: 'فبوضع ذا زكن رفع لذاك دون عكس' meaning by affirming one, the denial of the other is known, but not vice versa.
This is because both could be false.
3
Understand the rule for man' al-khuluww
The text says: 'فهو عكس ذا' meaning it is the converse: denying one affirms the other, but affirming one does not deny the other.
Because both could be true.
4
Recognize the complete disjunction
The text implies that for the complete disjunction (man' al-jam' wa al-khuluww), both rules apply: affirming one denies the other, and denying one affirms the other.
This is the common case in logic.

The Syllogism

Conditions and Moods of the Four Figures of Categorical Syllogism
Author enumerates the four figures of categorical syllogism with their conditions and productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for each of the four figures of categorical syllogism and determine the number of productive moods for each.

Core Idea
The four figures are ordered by perfection; their conditions ensure valid deduction.

This passage presents a compact versified summary of the four figures of categorical syllogism. Each figure is defined by the position of the middle term in the premises and has specific conditions that must be met for the syllogism to be productive. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four figures by middle term position
The first figure has the middle term as subject in the major and predicate in the minor. The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. The third has it as subject in both. The fourth is the converse of the first (middle term as predicate in the major and subject in the minor).
2
Memorize the conditions for each figure
First: minor affirmative, major universal. Second: premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and major universal. Third: both premises affirmative and at least one universal. Fourth: avoid both premises being negative and particular, except in the specific mood where minor affirmative particular and major negative universal.
These conditions are axioms for syllogistic validity.
3
Learn the number of productive moods per figure
First figure has 4 productive moods, second also 4, third has 6, and fourth has 5. Any combination not meeting the conditions is non-productive.
The exact moods can be derived but are not listed here.
4
Apply the 'inferior premise' rule for conclusion
The conclusion follows the 'inferior' (weaker) premise: if one premise is particular, conclusion is particular; if one is negative, conclusion is negative.
This rule applies to all figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَانِياً» عُرِفْ وَوَضْعُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَالِثاً» أُلِفْ (۸۷) وَرَابِعُ الأَشْكَالِ : عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ «وَهْيَ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي التَّكَمُّلِ (۸۸) فَحَيْثُ عَنْ هَذَا النِّظَامِ يُعْدَلُ فَفَاسِدُ النِّظَامِ ; أَمَّا الأَوَّلُ (۸۹) فَشَرْطُهُ : الإِيجَابُ فِي صُغْرَاهُ وَأَنْ تُرَى كُلِّيَّةٌ كُبْرَاهُ (٩٠) وَالثَّانِ : أَنْ يَخْتَلِفَا فِي الكَيْفِ مَعْ كُلِّيَّةِ الكُبْرَى لَهُ شَرْطُ وَقَعْ (۹۱) وَالثَّالِثُ : الإِيجَابُ فِي صُغْرَاهُمَا وَأَنْ تُرَى كُلِّيَّةً إِحْدَاهُمَا (۹۲) وَرَابِعُ : عَدَمُ جَمْعِ الخِسَتَيْنْ ; إِلَّا بِصُورَةٍ فَفِيهَا يَسْتَبِينْ (۹۳) صُغْرَاهُمَا مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّا كُبْرَاهُمَا سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّة (۹٤) فَمُنْتِجُ لِـ «أَوَّلِ» : أَرْبَعَةُ، كَـ «الثَّانِ»، ثُمَّ «ثَالِثُ»: فَسِتَّةُ (۹٥) وَرَابِعُ»: بِخَمْسَةٍ قَدْ أَنْتَجَا وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا (۹٦) وَتَتْبَعُ النَّتِيجَةُ الأَخَسَّ مِنْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ هَكَذَا زُكِنْ (۹۷) وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ بِالحَمْلِي مُخْتَصَّةٌ وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِيّ (۹۸) وَالحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَوِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِعِلْمِ آتِ (۹۹) وَتَنْتَهِي إِلَى ضَرُورَةٍ؛ لِمَا مِنْ دَوْرٍ أَوْ تَسَلْسُل قَدْ لَزِمَا
And its (the middle term) being a predicate in the universal (premise) is known as 'second' (figure), and its being a subject in the universal (premise) is composed as 'third'. And the fourth of the figures is the converse of the first. They are in order of perfection. So wherever one deviates from this system, it is a corrupt system. As for the first, its condition is affirmation in its minor and that its major be seen as universal. And the second: that they differ in quality with universality of the major; for it a condition has occurred. And the third: affirmation in its two (premises) and that one of them be seen as universal. And the fourth: the absence of combining the two deficiencies except in a form in which it becomes clear: its two (premises): the minor affirmative particular, the major negative universal. So the productive (moods) for the first are four, like the second, then the third: six, and the fourth: with five have produced. And other than what I mentioned will not produce. And the conclusion follows the inferior of those premises; thus it is known. And these figures are specific to categorical (syllogism) and not to conditional. And omission (of a premise or conclusion) in some premises or the conclusion for a knowledge to come. And they end in necessity, because of no circularity or infinite regress that would have occurred.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a compact versified summary of the categorical syllogism's four figures, their conditions, and the number of productive moods. The author begins by describing the second and third figures based on the position of the middle term: in the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises; in the third, it is the subject of both. The fourth figure is simply the converse of the first. The figures are ordered by their degree of perfection, the first being the most perfect. The first figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative and the major premise universal. The second figure requires the two premises to differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major to be universal. The third requires both premises affirmative and at least one universal. The fourth avoids combining two deficiencies (i.e., both premises particular and negative) except in the specific mood: minor affirmative particular (e.g., 'Some A are B') and major negative universal (e.g., 'No C is B'), which is productive. The author then gives the number of productive moods: First figure has 4, second also 4, third has 6, fourth has 5. Any other combination is non-productive. The conclusion always follows the 'inferior' (weaker) of the premises: if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if one is negative, the conclusion is negative. These rules apply only to categorical syllogisms, not conditional ones. The passage ends by noting that sometimes a premise or conclusion may be omitted for brevity, but the chain of reasoning must end in necessity, avoiding circularity or infinite regress.

Rules of the Conjunctive Conditional (Muttasil)
Rules for muttasil conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid inferences in a conjunctive conditional argument using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
In muttasil, only two moods are valid: modus ponens and modus tollens.

The conjunctive conditional is the foundation of if-then reasoning. In Islamic logic, it is used to derive consequences from premises, for example in kalam arguments. This lesson teaches the two valid inference patterns and warns against the fallacies of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent.

Guided Path
1
Find the conditional premise
Identify the 'if...then' statement (al-shartiyya al-muttasila). Note which part is the antecedent (muqaddam) and which is the consequent (tali).
2
Check the exception (istithna')
The second premise either affirms or denies one part. Determine whether it affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent. These are the two valid options.
If the second premise affirms the consequent or denies the antecedent, the inference is invalid.
3
Derive the conclusion
If the antecedent is affirmed, conclude the consequent. If the consequent is denied, conclude the denial of the antecedent. This follows the author's rule: وضع ذاك وضع الصالي, and رفع تال رفع أول.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Example: 'If an act leads to injustice, it is forbidden. This act leads to injustice. Therefore, it is forbidden.' This is valid modus ponens. Try a fallacious one: 'If it is a calamity, then it is from Allah. It is from Allah. Therefore it is a calamity.' This is invalid (affirming the consequent).
Use examples from tafsir or fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي) (١٠٠) وَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بـ: الاسْتِثْنَائِي) يُعْرَفُ بِـ : «الشَّرْطِيْ بِلَا امْتِرَاءِ (١٠١) وَهُوَ : الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيْجَةِ أَوْ ضِدِّهَا بِالفِعْلِ لَا بِالقُوَّةِ (١٠٢) فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا اتِّصَالِ : أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ الصَّالِي (١٠٣) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلٍ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى (١٠٤) وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً : فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا (١٠٥) وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصَّ ، ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ (١٠٦) رَفْعُ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ، وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا
Chapter on the exceptive (syllogism). (100) And from it is what is called the exceptive; it is known by 'conditional' without doubt. (101) And it is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially. (102) So if the conditional is conjunctive: positing that (antecedent) produces positing the consequent, and removing the consequent removes the antecedent; but the converse of these two does not follow, as is clear. (104) And if it is disjunctive: then positing this produces removing that, and the converse is likewise. (105) And that is in the exclusive (exclusive disjunction). Then if it is a preventer of combination, then by positing this (one disjunct) it is known (106) to remove that (the other) without converse; and when it is a preventer of removal, then it is the converse of this.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the rules for conditional syllogisms, dividing them into two types: conjunctive (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil). The author first defines an 'exceptive syllogism' (qiyas istithna'i) as a conditional syllogism that actually produces the conclusion or its opposite, not merely potentially. For conjunctive conditionals (if-then), the valid moods are: affirming the antecedent necessitates affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent necessitates denying the antecedent. The converse (affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent) is invalid. For disjunctive conditionals (either-or), the author distinguishes between two subtypes: exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluww) and preventing-of-combination (man'at al-jam'). In exclusive disjunction (where exactly one is true), affirming one disjunct produces denying the other, and denying one produces affirming the other. In preventing-of-combination (where both cannot be true but both can be false), affirming one produces denying the other, but denying one does not produce affirming the other. Conversely, if it is a preventing-of-removal (man'at al-khuluw, where at least one must be true), then denying one produces affirming the other. The text uses concise notation: 'ذا' (this) and 'ذاك' (that) refer to the two parts of the conditional.

شرطي
متصل
مقدم
تالي
وضع
رفع

Key Takeaway
In a conjunctive conditional, valid inferences are modus ponens and modus tollens; the converses are invalid.

Rules of the Disjunctive Conditional (Munfasil)
Rules for munfasil conditional syllogism and its subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two subtypes of disjunctive conditionals (man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw) and apply the appropriate inference rules.

Core Idea
In disjunctive syllogisms, the inference rules depend on whether the disjunction is exclusive, prevents combination, or prevents removal.

Disjunctive conditionals (either-or) appear in Islamic arguments where a choice must be made between two options. The type of disjunction determines which inferences are valid. This lesson clarifies the three main types and their rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjuncts
A disjunctive conditional has two parts (disjuncts). Determine what they are. Example: 'Either the world is created or uncreated.'
2
Determine the type of disjunction
Check if the disjunction is exclusive (one must be true, both cannot be true) or if it prevents combination (both cannot be true but both can be false) or prevents removal (at least one must be true but both can be true). The author mentions 'الأخص' for exclusive, 'مانع جمع' for preventer of combination, and 'مانع رفع' for preventer of removal.
The text says 'وذاك في الأخص' referring to the rule that both positing and denying work for exclusive disjunction.
3
Apply the rule for exclusive disjunction
If exactly one must be true, then affirming one implies denying the other, and denying one implies affirming the other. This is the rule in line 105: وضع ذا ينتج رفع ذاك والعكس كذا.
4
Apply the rule for preventing combination
If both cannot be true (but can be false), then affirming one implies denying the other, but denying one does not imply affirming the other. The author says: 'بِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ رَفْعُ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ'.
5
Apply the rule for preventing removal
If at least one must be true (both can be true), then denying one implies affirming the other, but affirming one does not imply denying the other. The author says: 'وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا' which is the converse of the preventing combination rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي) (١٠٠) وَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بـ: الاسْتِثْنَائِي) يُعْرَفُ بِـ : «الشَّرْطِيْ بِلَا امْتِرَاءِ (١٠١) وَهُوَ : الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيْجَةِ أَوْ ضِدِّهَا بِالفِعْلِ لَا بِالقُوَّةِ (١٠٢) فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا اتِّصَالِ : أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ الصَّالِي (١٠٣) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلٍ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى (١٠٤) وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً : فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا (١٠٥) وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصَّ ، ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ (١٠٦) رَفْعُ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ، وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا
Chapter on the exceptive (syllogism). (100) And from it is what is called the exceptive; it is known by 'conditional' without doubt. (101) And it is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially. (102) So if the conditional is conjunctive: positing that (antecedent) produces positing the consequent, and removing the consequent removes the antecedent; but the converse of these two does not follow, as is clear. (104) And if it is disjunctive: then positing this produces removing that, and the converse is likewise. (105) And that is in the exclusive (exclusive disjunction). Then if it is a preventer of combination, then by positing this (one disjunct) it is known (106) to remove that (the other) without converse; and when it is a preventer of removal, then it is the converse of this.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the rules for conditional syllogisms, dividing them into two types: conjunctive (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil). The author first defines an 'exceptive syllogism' (qiyas istithna'i) as a conditional syllogism that actually produces the conclusion or its opposite, not merely potentially. For conjunctive conditionals (if-then), the valid moods are: affirming the antecedent necessitates affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent necessitates denying the antecedent. The converse (affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent) is invalid. For disjunctive conditionals (either-or), the author distinguishes between two subtypes: exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluww) and preventing-of-combination (man'at al-jam'). In exclusive disjunction (where exactly one is true), affirming one disjunct produces denying the other, and denying one produces affirming the other. In preventing-of-combination (where both cannot be true but both can be false), affirming one produces denying the other, but denying one does not produce affirming the other. Conversely, if it is a preventing-of-removal (man'at al-khuluw, where at least one must be true), then denying one produces affirming the other. The text uses concise notation: 'ذا' (this) and 'ذاك' (that) refer to the two parts of the conditional.

Key Terms
شرطي
منفصل
مانع جمع
مانع رفع
أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Affirming one disjunct denies the other.
B) Denying one disjunct affirms the other.
C) Both A and B are valid.
D) Neither is valid.

Key Takeaway
The validity of inferences in a disjunctive syllogism depends on the type of disjunction: exclusive, preventing combination, or preventing removal.

Constructing Compound Syllogisms (Qiyas Murakkab)
Author defines compound syllogism and describes how to chain multiple syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a compound syllogism by chaining simple syllogisms and identify whether the chain is continuous or discrete.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism chains conclusions as premises to extend reasoning indefinitely.

Compound syllogisms allow us to build complex arguments from simple ones. In this passage, the author explains how to take the conclusion of one syllogism and use it as a premise in another, creating a chain. This technique is essential for advanced theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the final conclusion
Start with the conclusion you want to prove in the overall argument. This will be the ultimate goal of the compound syllogism.
2
Break down into intermediate conclusions
Determine what intermediate conclusions are needed to reach the final one. Each intermediate conclusion will be proven by a simple syllogism.
3
Construct simple syllogisms for each intermediate
For each intermediate conclusion, ensure it follows validly from premises using one of the figures of categorical syllogism.
4
Chain the syllogisms
Take the conclusion of the first syllogism and use it as a premise in the next syllogism. Continue until the final conclusion is reached.
5
Classify the chain as continuous or discrete
If each conclusion is immediately used as a premise in the next syllogism, it is continuous (mutassil). If conclusions are not directly linked but still valid in sequence, it is discrete (mafsul). Both are equally valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهُ مَا يَدْعُونَهُ: «مُرَكَّبَا» لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ حُجَجٍ قَدْرُكْبَا (۱۰۸) فَرَكْبَتُهُ إِنْ تُرِدْ أَنْ تَعْلَمَهُ وَاقْلِبْ نَتِيجَةٌ بِهِ مُقَدِّمَهُ (١٠٩) يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَرْكِيبِهَا بِأُخْرَى نَتِيجَةٌ إِلَى هَلُمَّ جَرًا (۱۱۰) مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِحِ الَّذِي حَوَى يَكُونُ، أَوْ مَفْصُولَها كُلِّ سَوَا
And from it is what they call 'compound', because it is composed of multiple arguments. (108) So its composition, if you wish to know it: take a conclusion and make it a premise (in another syllogism). (109) From combining it with another (premise) a conclusion necessarily follows, and so on. (110) It may be with the conclusions connected (continuous) that it contains, or separated (discrete); all are equal (in validity).
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of a compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab), which is a chain of two or more syllogisms where the conclusion of one syllogism becomes a premise for the next. The author instructs that to understand its composition, one takes a conclusion and uses it as a premise in another syllogism. From combining that premise with another premise, a new conclusion follows, and this process can continue indefinitely. The chain can be 'mutassil al-nata'ij' (where the conclusions are consecutively linked, meaning each conclusion is immediately used as a premise) or 'mafsul al-nata'ij' (where the conclusions are separate, i.e., not directly linked but still valid). Both types are equally valid as long as each step is sound.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism chains multiple simple syllogisms by using each conclusion as a premise in the next, and both continuous and discrete chains are valid.

Errors in Demonstration: Material and Formal Fallacies
Author enumerates material and formal errors that corrupt demonstrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify errors in demonstrations as material (in expression or meaning) or formal (violation of syllogistic figures) and identify specific types such as ambiguity, confusion of accidental with essential, and illicit structural moves.

Core Idea
Demonstration fails either from false premises (material) or faulty structure (formal).

This concluding passage of al-Akhdari's poem on logic summarizes the two fundamental sources of error in demonstrative reasoning: material and formal. Understanding this classification is essential for any student of logic because it provides the diagnostic framework for critiquing arguments. We will unpack each category and the examples given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main error types
The author states that error in demonstration (khaṭa' al-burhān) occurs either in matter (mādda) or in form (ṣūra). These are the two broad categories.
Arabic terms: مَادَّة (madda), صُورَة (sura)
2
Subdivide material errors
Material errors are further divided into errors in expression (lafẓ) and errors in meaning (maʿnā). Expression errors include ambiguity (ishtirāk) and false synonymy (radīf). Meaning errors include: confusing accidental (ʿaraḍī) with essential (dhātī), premises that do not produce the conclusion, judging a genus by the ruling of its species, and treating non-certain premises as certain.
Memorize the five listed meaning errors.
3
Recognize formal errors
Formal errors are those that violate the rules of syllogistic figures (ashkāl) or abandon the conditions required for a valid conclusion. There are no subdivisions; it is a single category of structural invalidity.
Formal errors are about the shape of the syllogism, not the truth of premises.
4
Apply the classification
Given an argument, first check whether the error lies in the content (premises: their truth, relevance, or clarity) or in the logical structure (figure, mood, conversion rules). Then, if material, decide if it is an expression or meaning error.
Practice by classifying the example in the checkpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(خَاتِمَةٌ)
وَخَطَأُ البُرْهَانِ حَيْثُ وُجِدَا: فِي مَادَةٍ، أَوْ صُورَةٍ فَالمُبْتَدَا في «اللَّفْظِ كَ: اشْتِرَاكِ، أَوْ كَ: جَعْلِ ذَا تَبَايُنَ مِثْلَ الرَّدِيفِ مَأَخَذَا وَفِي المَعَانِي»: لالْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَهُ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ، فَافْهَمِ المُخَاطَبَهُ كَ: مِثْلِ جَعَلِ الْعَرَضِيْ كَالذَّاتِي أَوْنَاتِجِ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَالحُكْمِ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ وَجَعْلِ كَالقَطْعِيِّ غَيْرِ القَطْعِي وَ الثَّانِكَ: الخُرُوجِ عَنْ أَشْكَالِهِ وَتَرْكِ شَرْطِ النَّتْحِ مِنْ إِكْمَالِهِ هَذَا تَمَامُ الغَرَضِ المَقْصُودِ مِنْ أُمَّهَاتِ المَنْطِقِ المَحْمُودِ
Conclusion: And the error of demonstration wherever found: in matter or in form, thus the beginning. In expression: like ambiguity, or like making this distinction as if it were a synonym taken. And in meanings: due to confusion of falsehood with truth itself, so understand the address. Like making the accidental as the essential, or one of the premises not producing the conclusion, and judging the genus by the judgment of the species, and making the non-certain as if it were certain. And the second: exiting from its figures and abandoning the condition of the conclusion from its completion. This is the completion of the intended purpose from the mother books of commendable logic.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a critical conclusion (khatima) on errors in demonstration (burhan). The author divides errors into two main categories: material (madda) and formal (sura). Material errors occur either in expression (lafz) or in meaning (ma'na). Examples of expression errors include ambiguity (ishtirak) and false synonymy (radif). Meaning errors include: confusing accidental (aradi) with essential (dhati), premises that do not yield the conclusion, judging a genus by a property of its species, and treating non-certain premises as certain. Formal errors (the second type) involve violating the rules of syllogistic figures (ashkal) or failing to meet the conditions for a valid conclusion. The author then concludes the poem, stating that this completes the intended purpose from the commendable logic books.

The Syllogism

The Two Conditions of a Valid Syllogism
Author defines qiyas and explains its two conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two conditions of a valid syllogism (taslim and bi-dhatihi) and apply them to determine whether a given argument qualifies as a qiyas in the technical sense.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism does not require true premises—only accepted ones—and the conclusion must follow from the premises alone.

In this passage, the author unpacks the definition of qiyas (syllogism) and clarifies two critical conditions often misunderstood by beginners. First, the premises need only be conceded by the interlocutor (taslim); their factual truth is irrelevant to the logical validity of the inference. Second, the entailment must be intrinsic to the premises (bi-dhatihi), not dependent on an external principle or rule. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qiyas
Locate the definition in the opening line: 'Verily, the syllogism is composed of propositions arranged as an entailment by itself of another statement.' Note the key components: it is composed of propositions, entails another statement, and does so by itself.
The phrase 'by itself' (bi-dhatihi) is the condition we will examine in step 3.
2
Understand the taslim condition
The author explains that the truth of the premises is not required; only their acceptance (taslim) by the opponent matters. He gives an example: even if someone says 'The world is eternal' and 'Everything eternal has a creator' (both false), the conclusion 'The world has a creator' follows logically from these accepted premises.
This shows logical validity is independent of factual truth.
3
Understand the bi-dhatihi condition
The condition 'by themselves' excludes cases where the entailment relies on an external rule. The author illustrates with the equality example: from 'Zayd is equal to Amr' and 'Amr is equal to Bakr,' we conclude 'Zayd is equal to Bakr' only because of the external rule that 'the equal of the equal to something is equal to that thing.' If we replace 'equal' with 'enemy,' no such conclusion follows. Thus, the entailment must be intrinsic to the premises.
In a valid qiyas, the middle term must appear in both premises, making the inference direct without external help.
4
Apply both conditions to the given examples
Examine the first example (world/eternal/creator): are the premises accepted? Yes, in the hypothetical. Does the conclusion follow by itself? Yes, because the middle term 'eternal' connects directly. Now examine the equality example: does it satisfy bi-dhatihi? No, because an external rule is needed. Therefore, it is not a valid qiyas in the technical sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ القِيَاسَ مِنْ قَضَايَا صُوِّرًا مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَا وَإِنَّمَا قَالُوا : مِنْ تَسْلِيم ... إلخ»؛ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ حِقِّيَّتُهُمَا (۱)(۲) ، بَلِ المَدَارُ عَلَى تَسْلِيمِهِمَا ، وَلَوْ كَانَا جَهْلاً ، كَمَا لَوْ قَالَ قَائِلٌ : العَالَمُ قَدِيمٌ، وَكُلُّ مَنْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ موجد»، فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمٍ هَذَيْنِ القَوْلَيْنِ - مَعَ كَوْنِهِمَا جَهْلاً فِي الوَاقِعِ - أَنْ يُقَالَ: «العَالَمُ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُوجِده . وَخَرَجَ بِقَيْدِ «لِذَاتِهِمَا » : القَوْلُ اللَّازِمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمِ قَوْلَينِ لَا لِذَاتِهِمَا، بَلْ لِأَمْرٍ خَارِج؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِمْ : زَيْدٌ مُسَاوِ لِعَمْرٍو، وَعَمْرُو مُسَاءٍ لِبَكْرِه (۳) ، فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَسْلِيمٍ هَذَيْنِ القَوْلَيْنِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: زَيْدٌ مُسَاءٍ لِبَكْرٍ لَكِنْ بِوَاسِطَةِ أَمْرٍ خَارِجٍ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ القَاعِدَةَ : أَنَّ مُسَاوِيَ المُسَاوِي لِشَيْءٍ، مُسَاوِ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء» ؛ بِدَلِيلِ أَنَّكَ لَوْ أَبْدَلْتَ مَادَّةَ المُسَاوَاةِ بِمَادَّةِ العَدَاوَةِ مَثَلاً (۲) ، وَقُلْتَ : زَيْدٌ عَدُوٌّ لِعَمْرِو ، وَعَمْرُو عَدُوٌّ لِبَكْرٍ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «زَيْدٌ عَدُوٌّ لِبَكْرِ».
Verily, the syllogism is composed of propositions arranged as an entailment by itself of another statement. And they said: 'from the acceptance... etc.' as an indication that their truth is not required, rather the consideration is on their acceptance, even if they are ignorant [false]. As if someone said: 'The world is eternal, and everything that is such must have a creator,' then from the acceptance of these two statements — despite their being false in reality — it follows that it is said: 'The world must have a creator.' And by the restriction 'by themselves' is excluded: a statement entailed from the acceptance of two statements not by themselves but by an external factor; as in their saying: 'Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr,' for from the acceptance of these two statements it follows that it is said: 'Zayd is equal to Bakr,' but through an external factor, which is the rule: 'The equal of the equal to something is equal to that thing.' Evidence being that if you replace the matter of equality with the matter of enmity, for example, and say: 'Zayd is an enemy of Amr, and Amr is an enemy of Bakr,' it does not follow that it is said: 'Zayd is an enemy of Bakr.'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage explains the definition of qiyas (syllogism) and its two essential conditions: first, that the premises need only be accepted (taslim) by the interlocutor, not necessarily true in reality; second, that the entailment must be by the premises themselves (bi-dhatihima) and not due to an external rule. The author begins by quoting the matn line that the syllogism is composed of propositions that entail another statement by themselves. He then clarifies that the phrase 'from acceptance' (min taslim) indicates that the truth of the premises is not required; the logical operation relies solely on the opponent's concession. He gives the example of someone saying 'The world is eternal' and 'Everything eternal has a creator' — both false in reality — yet from accepting them, we validly conclude that the world has a creator. This shows that logical validity is independent of factual truth. Next, he explains the condition 'by themselves' (bi-dhatihima). If the entailment is not due to the premises themselves but to an external principle, it is not a valid syllogism. The example of equality illustrates this: from 'Zayd is equal to Amr' and 'Amr is equal to Bakr' we conclude 'Zayd is equal to Bakr', but this inference relies on the external rule of transitivity of equality, not on the inherent connection of the two statements. To prove this, the author substitutes 'enmity' for 'equality' and shows that no similar conclusion follows. Thus, true qiyas requires that the middle term be repeated in the two premises, making the entailment intrinsic. This distinction is crucial in logic: the syllogism's form must guarantee the conclusion solely by its structure, not by hidden assumptions.

Key Terms
قياس
تسليم
بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The premises must be true in reality.
B) The conclusion must follow from the premises alone without an external rule.
C) The argument must have three terms.
D) The premises must be accepted by both parties.

Key Takeaway
A valid qiyas requires only that the premises be accepted, not true, and that the conclusion follows intrinsically from the premises themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following argument: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, nabidh is haram.' Does this argument satisfy the condition of bi-dhatihi? Explain why or why not, referring to the author's condition that the entailment must be by the premises themselves.

The Syllogism

Deriving Particular Rulings from Universal Propositions
Author demonstrates how universal propositions yield rulings for particulars through syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid categorical syllogism that derives a specific ruling for a particular from a universal proposition in an Islamic science context.

Core Idea
Universal propositions unleash their ruling power only through the syllogism applied to each particular.

This lesson shows you the exact mechanical procedure to take a universal rule—like 'Every fa'il is marfu''—and generate a concrete ruling for any instance, such as 'Zayd is marfu''. The author walks you through the syllogistic structure step by step, using both a grammatical and a logical example. By mastering this method, you will be able to systematically apply universal principles across the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal proposition and its parts
Locate the universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya). It has a subject (mawdu') and a predicate (mahmul). Note also the ruling (hukm) affirmed or denied. For example: 'Every fa'il is marfu'' has subject 'fa'il' and predicate 'marfu''.
The universal proposition will serve as the major premise (kubra) of your syllogism.
2
Pick a particular and form the minor premise
Choose a particular instance of the universal's subject—say 'Zayd' from 'Zayd stood'. Now form a proposition where this particular is the subject, and the universal's subject ('fa'il') is the predicate. This gives 'Zayd is a fa'il'. This is your minor premise (sughra).
The minor premise connects the particular to the universal's subject, which will then link to the ruling via the major premise.
3
Combine premises into a categorical syllogism
Place the minor premise first, then the major premise. The syllogism is: Zayd is a fa'il (minor); Every fa'il is marfu' (major). The middle term (hadd al-awsat) is 'fa'il'—it appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
This is a first-figure syllogism (shakl awwal) because the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
4
Extract and verify the conclusion
From the two premises, the conclusion follows necessarily: 'Zayd is marfu''. The subject of the conclusion is the particular, the predicate is the universal's predicate. Check that the syllogism is valid: all premises are true, and the form is Barbara (AAA-1).
The author explicitly says 'the conclusion emerges speaking with the ruling of that particular'.
5
Apply the same method to logical or fiqh examples
Repeat the process with any universal rule. For example, take 'Every universal predicated of many differing in reality is a genus'. To show that 'animal' is a genus, form: 'Animal is a universal predicated of many differing in reality' (minor), and the universal rule (major), yielding 'Animal is a genus'.
The method works identically whether the science is grammar, logic, fiqh, or kalam.
