⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمَوْضُوعُ هَذِهِ الْقَضِيَّةِ: الْفَاعِلُ. وَجُزْئِيَّاتُهُ: زَيْدٌ مِنْ قَامَ زَيْدٌ، وَعَمْرٌو مِنْ جَاءَ عَمْرٌو، وَبَكْرٌ مِنْ نَامَ بَكْرٌ. وَنَحْوُهَا. وَأَحْكَامُهَا: ثُبُوتُ الرَّفْعِ. وَكَيْفِيَّةُ تَعَرُّفِ أَحْكَامِهَا مِنَ الْقَضِيَّةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ: أَنْ تَجْعَلَ الْجُزْئِيَّ الَّذِي تُرِيدُ مَعْرِفَةَ حُكْمِهِ مَوْضُوعاً، وَتَجْعَلَ مَوْضُوعَ الْقَضِيَّةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ مَحْمُولاً، وَتَجْعَلَ الْقَضِيَّةَ الْمُرَكَّبَةَ مِنْهُمَا صُغْرَى، ثُمَّ تَجْعَلَ الْقَضِيَّةَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ كُبْرَى، فَإِذَا رَكَّبْتَهُمَا قِيَاساً خَرَجَتِ النَّتِيجَةُ نَاطِقَةٌ بِحُكْمِ ذَلِكَ الْجُزْئِيِّ. فَإِذَا قُلْتَ فِي الْمِثَالِ الْمَذْكُورِ: زَيْدٌ فَاعِلٌ، وَكُلُّ فَاعِلٍ مَرْفُوعٌ، خَرَجَتِ النَّتِيجَةُ قَائِلَةٌ: زَيْدٌ مَرْفُوعٌ. وَكَقَوْلِهِمْ فِي هَذَا الْفَنِّ: كُلُّ كُلِّيٍّ مَقُولٍ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ جِنْسٌ: فَمَوْضُوعُ هَذِهِ الْقَضِيَّةِ: الْكُلِّيُّ الْمَذْكُورُ. وَجُزْئِيَّاتُهُ: حَيَوَانٌ، وَجِسْمٌ، وَجَوْهَرٌ، وَنَحْوُهَا مِنَ الْأَجْنَاسِ. وَأَحْكَامُهَا: كَوْنُهَا أَجْنَاساً. وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ كَيْفِيَّةَ تَعَرُّفِ أَحْكَامِهَا مِنْهَا، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ: الْحَيَوَانُ كُلِّيٌّ مَقُولٌ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ، وَكُلُّ كُلِّيٍّ مَقُولٍ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ جِنْسٌ، خَرَجَتِ النَّتِيجَةُ قَائِلَةٌ: الْحَيَوَانُ جِنْسٌ. وَعَلَى هَذَا الْقِيَاسُ.
So the subject of this proposition is 'al-fa'il'. Its particulars are: Zayd from 'Zayd stood', and 'Amr from 'Amr came', and Bakr from 'Bakr slept', and the like. And its rulings are: the affirmation of raf'. And the manner of knowing its rulings from the universal proposition is: that you make the particular whose ruling you want to know the subject, and you make the subject of the universal proposition the predicate, and you make the composite proposition from them the minor premise, then you make the universal proposition the major premise; when you combine them as a syllogism, the conclusion emerges speaking with the ruling of that particular. So if you say in the mentioned example: 'Zayd is a fa'il', and 'Every fa'il is marfu'', the conclusion emerges saying: 'Zayd is marfu''. And likewise their statement in this art: 'Every universal predicated of many differing in reality is a genus': so the subject of this proposition is the mentioned 'universal'. Its particulars are: animal, body, substance, and the like from the genera. And its rulings are: their being genera. And you have known the manner of knowing its rulings from it, so if you say: 'Animal is a universal predicated of many differing in reality, and every universal predicated of many differing in reality is a genus', the conclusion emerges saying: 'Animal is a genus'. And this is the analogy.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a practical demonstration of how a universal proposition (qadiyya kulliyya) is applied to derive rulings for its particulars using a categorical syllogism. The author takes two examples: one from Arabic grammar and one from logic. For the grammar example, the universal proposition is 'Every fa'il is marfu'. Its subject is 'fa'il', its particulars are instances like Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and its ruling is the affirmation of raf' (the nominative case). To derive a ruling for a particular, say Zayd, the method is: take Zayd as subject (subject of minor premise), take the universal's subject ('fa'il') as predicate for that minor (i.e., 'Zayd is a fa'il'), then take the universal proposition ('Every fa'il is marfu') as major premise. The syllogism is: Zayd is a fa'il (minor); every fa'il is marfu' (major); therefore Zayd is marfu'. The author then applies the same method to a logical definition: 'Every universal predicated of many differing in reality is a genus' is the universal. Its subject is that universal, its particulars are 'animal', 'body', 'substance', etc., and its ruling is that they are genera. To show that animal is a genus, we construct: animal is a universal predicated of many differing in reality (minor); every such universal is a genus (major); therefore animal is a genus. This clearly illustrates the syllogistic use of universal propositions to establish rulings for particular cases, a method central to Islamic logic and used across the sciences.

C) Zayd is a fa'il

Key Takeaway
A universal proposition is not applied directly; it must be combined with a minor premise linking a particular to the universal's subject in a categorical syllogism to yield a concrete ruling.

Purified Logic: Its Permissibility and Obligation
Author classifies logic and rules on its study

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of logic according to the author and state the legal ruling for each type, including the condition for obligatory status.

Core Idea
Logic is not monolithic; only purified logic is a communal obligation.

The author divides logic into two categories based on content: one free from philosophical errors, the other containing them. This lesson clarifies the ruling for each type and explains why the early Muslim community did not need formal logic. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly applying the discipline in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the first part of the Arabic passage. Note that the author distinguishes logic free from misguidance (khāl 'an ḍalālāt al-falāsifa) from logic that is not free from those errors. List the examples of books belonging to the first type.
The second type refers to the books of the earlier philosophers (mutqaddimīn).
2
Extract the ruling for each type
For the first type, the author says there is no disagreement on its permissibility; rather it is a communal obligation (fard kifāya). For the second type, the author says it is the one concerning which there is disagreement. Understand that the ruling is not uniform.
The dispute about the second type arises because it contains potentially heretical content.
3
Analyze the condition for obligation
The passage states that the obligation applies only when one cannot dispense with logic through innate intelligence and sound natural disposition. Find the phrase 'idhā lam yastaghni 'anhu bi-jūdati al-dhihn wa-ṣiḥḥati al-ṭab‘' and note that the early generations did not need formal logic for this reason.
This condition is mentioned by al-Sanūsī and others.
4
Apply the rule to contemporary study
Consider: Given that most people nowadays do not possess the sharp intellect of the Companions, studying purified logic becomes a communal obligation. Reflect on how this reasoning applies to your own context.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ عِلْمَ المَنْطِقِ قِسْمَانِ: (١) - أَحَدُهُمَا: مَا هُوَ خَالٍ عَنْ ضَلَالَاتِ الفَلَاسِفَةِ المُكَفِّرَةِ وَغَيْرِهَا كَ: المَذْكُورِ فِي هَذَا المَتْنِ، وَمُخْتَصَرِ السَّنُوسِيِّ، وَإِيْسَاغُوجِي، وَمُخْتَصَرِ ابْنِ عَرَفَة وَتَألِيفِ الكَاتِبِيِّ وَالخُوْنَجِيِّ وَالسَّعْدِ وَغَيْرِهِمْ مِنَ المُتَأَخْرِينَ. وَهَذَا القِسْمُ لَا خِلَافَ فِي جَوَازِ الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ، بَلْ هُوَ فَرْضُ كِفَايَةٍ عَلَى أَهْلِ كُلِّ إِقْلِيمٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ رَدُّ الشُّكُوكِ فِي عِلْمِ الكَلامِ ، وَهُوَ فَرْضُ كِفَايَةٍ، وَمَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، وَمَحَلُّ ذَلِكَ إِذَا لَمْ يَسْتَغْنِ عَنْهُ بِجُودَةِ الذَّهْنِ وَصِحَّةِ الطَّبْعِ كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّنُوسِيُّ فِي شَرْحِ مُخْتَصَرِهِ وَابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ وَغَيْرُهُمَا ، وَلِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَحْتَجْ إِلَيْهِ الصَّحَابَةُ وَالتَّابِعُونَ وَالأَئِمَّةُ المُجْتَهِدُونَ وَأَصْحَابُهُمْ. (٢) - وَثَانِيهُمَا : مَا لَيْسَ خَالِياً عَنْ تِلْكَ الضَّلَالَاتِ كَالمَذْكُورِ فِي كُتُب المُتَقَدِّمِينَ، وَهَذَا القِسْمُ هُوَ الَّذِي فِيهِ الخِلَافُ. قَالَ الْأَنْبَابِي: وَفِي كَلَامِهِ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى قِيَاسٍ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ؛ نَظْمُهُ هَكَذَا : عِلْمُ المَنْطِقِ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ، وَكُلٌّ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، يَنْتِجُ : عِلْمُ المَنْطِقِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، وَهُوَ المُدَّعَى.
And know that the science of logic is two types: (1) One of them: what is free from the misguidance of the philosophers that leads to disbelief and other things, like what is mentioned in this text, and the Mukhtasar of al-Sanusi, and Isaghuji, and the Mukhtasar of Ibn 'Arafa, and the writings of al-Katibi, al-Khunaji, al-Sa'd, and others among the later scholars. This type: there is no disagreement about the permissibility of engaging in it, rather it is a communal obligation (fard kifaya) upon the people of every region; because the refutation of doubts in the science of Kalam depends on it, and Kalam is a communal obligation, and whatever a communal obligation depends on is itself a communal obligation. And that applies when one cannot dispense with it by sharpness of intellect and soundness of nature, as al-Sanusi stated in his commentary on his Mukhtasar, and Ibn Ya'qub and others. For that reason, the Companions, the Followers, the Mujtahid Imams, and their students did not need it. (2) And the second type: what is not free from those misguidances, like what is mentioned in the books of the earlier scholars, and this type is the one concerning which there is disagreement. Al-Anbabi said: And in his words there is an indication to a syllogism of the first figure; its arrangement is thus: The science of logic is that upon which the communal obligation depends, and everything upon which the communal obligation depends is itself a communal obligation, therefore the science of logic is a communal obligation, which is the claim.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage classifies the science of logic (ilm al-mantiq) into two categories based on content and ruling. The first category is logic that is free from the deviant and potentially heretical doctrines of the ancient philosophers (dalalat al-falasifa al-mukaffira). This includes standard texts like al-Sullam (the present matn), al-Sanusi's Mukhtasar, Isaghuji, etc. Studying this type is unanimously permissible and actually a communal obligation (fard kifaya) upon the people of every region. The reasoning is a syllogism: refuting doubts in Kalam depends on logic; Kalam is a communal obligation; therefore logic is a communal obligation (first figure categorical syllogism with a hypothetical major premise? Actually it is: Logic is that upon which fard kifaya depends; everything upon which fard kifaya depends is fard kifaya; so logic is fard kifaya). However, this obligation is conditional: it applies only when one cannot dispense with logic through innate intelligence and sound natural disposition. The passage notes that the early generations (Sahaba, Tabi'un, etc.) did not need formal logic because of their intellectual gifts. The second category is logic that contains those philosophical errors, which is the disputed type. Al-Anbabi's commentary explicitly extracts the syllogism (qiyas min al-shakl al-awwal) and presents it in standard form.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Extracting the Syllogism Behind the Ruling
Al-Anbabi extracts an implicit syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first-figure categorical syllogism from the passage and verify its validity according to the conditions of categorical logic.

Core Idea
The ruling on logic is derived via a first-figure syllogism: if A depends on B and A is obligatory, then B is obligatory.

Al-Anbabi notes that the author's argument implicitly contains a first-figure syllogism. This lesson breaks down that syllogism into its premises and conclusion, identifies the middle term, and checks its validity. Understanding this reasoning is key to seeing how Islamic jurists derive rulings using logical principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natīja)
Al-Anbabi states the conclusion: 'The science of logic is a communal obligation.' Find this in the Arabic (علم المنطق يكون فرض كفاية) and confirm it is the claim being proved.
2
Find the middle term (ḥadd al-awsaṭ)
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Look for a term that connects the other two. In the syllogism given, the middle term is 'that upon which the communal obligation depends' (ما يتوقف عليه فرض الكفاية) or more precisely 'depends on it'.
The middle term is 'al-muta'allaq bihi fard al-kifāya' (what the communal obligation depends on).
3
Arrange the premises and identify the figure
Write the two premises in standard order: (1) The science of logic is that upon which the communal obligation depends. (2) Everything upon which the communal obligation depends is itself a communal obligation. Notice that the middle term is the predicate in the first premise and the subject in the second premise. This ordering corresponds to the first figure (shakl awwal). Verify: major premise (with major term = 'communal obligation') is the second premise; minor premise is the first.
The major term is 'communal obligation' (fard kifāya); the minor term is 'science of logic'.
4
Check validity and mood
Determine the mood: The first premise is a universal affirmative (A): 'Logic is a thing on which obligation depends.' The second premise is also universal affirmative (A): 'Every thing on which obligation depends is obligatory.' Thus the mood is AAA, and the figure is first. The valid mood for first figure is AAA (Barbara), so the syllogism is valid.
Al-Anbabi implicitly recognizes this validity by presenting the syllogism as proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ عِلْمَ المَنْطِقِ قِسْمَانِ: (١) - أَحَدُهُمَا: مَا هُوَ خَالٍ عَنْ ضَلَالَاتِ الفَلَاسِفَةِ المُكَفِّرَةِ وَغَيْرِهَا كَ: المَذْكُورِ فِي هَذَا المَتْنِ، وَمُخْتَصَرِ السَّنُوسِيِّ، وَإِيْسَاغُوجِي، وَمُخْتَصَرِ ابْنِ عَرَفَة وَتَألِيفِ الكَاتِبِيِّ وَالخُوْنَجِيِّ وَالسَّعْدِ وَغَيْرِهِمْ مِنَ المُتَأَخْرِينَ. وَهَذَا القِسْمُ لَا خِلَافَ فِي جَوَازِ الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ، بَلْ هُوَ فَرْضُ كِفَايَةٍ عَلَى أَهْلِ كُلِّ إِقْلِيمٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ رَدُّ الشُّكُوكِ فِي عِلْمِ الكَلامِ ، وَهُوَ فَرْضُ كِفَايَةٍ، وَمَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، وَمَحَلُّ ذَلِكَ إِذَا لَمْ يَسْتَغْنِ عَنْهُ بِجُودَةِ الذَّهْنِ وَصِحَّةِ الطَّبْعِ كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّنُوسِيُّ فِي شَرْحِ مُخْتَصَرِهِ وَابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ وَغَيْرُهُمَا ، وَلِذَلِكَ لَمْ يَحْتَجْ إِلَيْهِ الصَّحَابَةُ وَالتَّابِعُونَ وَالأَئِمَّةُ المُجْتَهِدُونَ وَأَصْحَابُهُمْ. (٢) - وَثَانِيهُمَا : مَا لَيْسَ خَالِياً عَنْ تِلْكَ الضَّلَالَاتِ كَالمَذْكُورِ فِي كُتُب المُتَقَدِّمِينَ، وَهَذَا القِسْمُ هُوَ الَّذِي فِيهِ الخِلَافُ. قَالَ الْأَنْبَابِي: وَفِي كَلَامِهِ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى قِيَاسٍ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ؛ نَظْمُهُ هَكَذَا : عِلْمُ المَنْطِقِ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ، وَكُلٌّ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، يَنْتِجُ : عِلْمُ المَنْطِقِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، وَهُوَ المُدَّعَى.
And know that the science of logic is two types: (1) One of them: what is free from the misguidance of the philosophers that leads to disbelief and other things, like what is mentioned in this text, and the Mukhtasar of al-Sanusi, and Isaghuji, and the Mukhtasar of Ibn 'Arafa, and the writings of al-Katibi, al-Khunaji, al-Sa'd, and others among the later scholars. This type: there is no disagreement about the permissibility of engaging in it, rather it is a communal obligation (fard kifaya) upon the people of every region; because the refutation of doubts in the science of Kalam depends on it, and Kalam is a communal obligation, and whatever a communal obligation depends on is itself a communal obligation. And that applies when one cannot dispense with it by sharpness of intellect and soundness of nature, as al-Sanusi stated in his commentary on his Mukhtasar, and Ibn Ya'qub and others. For that reason, the Companions, the Followers, the Mujtahid Imams, and their students did not need it. (2) And the second type: what is not free from those misguidances, like what is mentioned in the books of the earlier scholars, and this type is the one concerning which there is disagreement. Al-Anbabi said: And in his words there is an indication to a syllogism of the first figure; its arrangement is thus: The science of logic is that upon which the communal obligation depends, and everything upon which the communal obligation depends is itself a communal obligation, therefore the science of logic is a communal obligation, which is the claim.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage classifies the science of logic (ilm al-mantiq) into two categories based on content and ruling. The first category is logic that is free from the deviant and potentially heretical doctrines of the ancient philosophers (dalalat al-falasifa al-mukaffira). This includes standard texts like al-Sullam (the present matn), al-Sanusi's Mukhtasar, Isaghuji, etc. Studying this type is unanimously permissible and actually a communal obligation (fard kifaya) upon the people of every region. The reasoning is a syllogism: refuting doubts in Kalam depends on logic; Kalam is a communal obligation; therefore logic is a communal obligation (first figure categorical syllogism with a hypothetical major premise? Actually it is: Logic is that upon which fard kifaya depends; everything upon which fard kifaya depends is fard kifaya; so logic is fard kifaya). However, this obligation is conditional: it applies only when one cannot dispense with logic through innate intelligence and sound natural disposition. The passage notes that the early generations (Sahaba, Tabi'un, etc.) did not need formal logic because of their intellectual gifts. The second category is logic that contains those philosophical errors, which is the disputed type. Al-Anbabi's commentary explicitly extracts the syllogism (qiyas min al-shakl al-awwal) and presents it in standard form.

Key Terms
علم المنطق
فرض كفاية
قياس الشكل الأول
الوسط

A) The science of logic
B) The communal obligation
C) That upon which the communal obligation depends
D) Refutation of doubts

Key Takeaway
The duty of logic is derived through a valid first-figure categorical syllogism that depends on the communal obligation of Kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the following statement from fiqh: 'Studying the Quran is obligatory because it is necessary for performing prayer, and prayer is obligatory.' Reconstruct it as a first-figure syllogism with premises and conclusion, then check its validity.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Identifying a First-Figure Categorical Syllogism: Logic as Communal Obligation
Anbabai formalizes the reasoning as a first-figure categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises, middle term, and figure of a categorical syllogism from a classical Islamic text and verify its validity.

Core Idea
A first-figure syllogism yields certain truth when the middle term is positioned correctly as subject and predicate.

In his commentary, Anbabai explicitly lays out the logical structure underlying the claim that logic is a communal obligation. He presents it as a first-figure categorical syllogism (qiyās min al-shakl al-awwal). This lesson will guide you through extracting the parts of this syllogism, checking its validity, and understanding why it is a powerful tool for proving Islamic rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (al-natīja)
Find the sentence that is being proven. In this passage, it is: 'The science of logic is a communal obligation' (ʿilm al-manṭiq yakūnu farḍa kifāya). This is the claim the syllogism is designed to establish.
The conclusion is often preceded by 'yuntiju' (results in) or 'fa-innahu'.
2
Locate the minor premise (al-muqaddima al-ṣughrā)
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion. Here the subject is 'the science of logic'. The minor premise is: 'The science of logic is that on which the communal obligation depends' (ʿilm al-manṭiq yatawaqqaf ʿalayhi farḍ al-kifāya). This premise places logic under the middle term.
The minor premise links the subject to the middle term.
3
Locate the major premise (al-muqaddima al-kubrā)
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. The predicate is 'communal obligation'. The major premise is: 'Everything on which the communal obligation depends is a communal obligation' (kull mā yatawaqqaf ʿalayhi farḍ al-kifāya yakūnu farḍa kifāya). This premise states the general rule.
The major premise links the middle term to the predicate.
4
Extract the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ)
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Here it is 'that on which the communal obligation depends' (mā yatawaqqaf ʿalayhi farḍ al-kifāya). It connects logic to communal obligation.
In a valid first-figure syllogism, the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise.
5
Check the figure
In this syllogism, the middle term is the predicate of the minor premise ('...is that on which...') and the subject of the major premise ('Everything on which...'). This is the defining characteristic of the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal).
First figure: middle term is subject of major, predicate of minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي كَلَامِهِ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى قِيَاسٍ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ؛ نَظْمُهُ هَكَذَا : عِلْمُ المَنْطِقِ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ، وَكُلٌّ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَيْهِ فَرْضُ الكِفَايَةِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، ينتج : عِلْمُ المَنْطِقِ يَكُونُ فَرْضَ كِفَايَةٍ، وَهُوَ المُدَّعَى.
And in his words is an indication to a syllogism from the first figure; its structure is thus: The science of logic is that on which the communal obligation depends, and everything on which the communal obligation depends is a communal obligation, resulting in: The science of logic is a communal obligation, and that is the claim.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage extracts an explicit categorical syllogism from the preceding argument. The commentator (Anbabai) identifies it as a first-figure syllogism (qiyās min al-shakl al-awwal). The structure is: Major premise: 'Everything on which the communal obligation depends is a communal obligation.' Minor premise: 'The science of logic is that on which the communal obligation depends.' Conclusion: 'The science of logic is a communal obligation.' In the first figure, the middle term (mā yatawaqqaf 'alayhi farḍ al-kifāya) is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. This syllogism is valid (Barbara in Aristotelian terms). The conclusion restates the claim (al-mudda'ā) that logic is a communal obligation.

Key Terms

نظمه
المدعى

Key Takeaway
Every first-figure syllogism with universal affirmative premises (Barbara) yields a valid and certain conclusion, as demonstrated in the proof that logic is a communal obligation.

The Two Types of Propositions: Conditional and Categorical
Author states the two types and gives their foundational definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a conditional proposition from a categorical proposition by identifying the form of judgment (conditional vs. predicative).

Core Idea
The difference is in the judgment form: conditional is tied to a condition, categorical is not.

In Islamic logic, every proposition fits into one of two fundamental categories: conditional or categorical. This distinction is the bedrock for building syllogisms. The author states the division clearly, and the commentator clarifies the definitions and the reason for the names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division mentioned in the text
Notice that the author says propositions are of two types: shartiyya and hamliyya. The commentator notes that 'thumma' here is just for sequential mention, not for ranking.
2
Examine the definition of conditional proposition
Read the definition: a judgment made in the manner of condition and dependency. The commentator adds that this includes both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals because they contain a conditional particle explicitly or virtually.
Example: 'If the number is even, it is not odd' is conditional.
3
Contrast with categorical definition
Now look at the categorical: a judgment made in the manner of predication (haml), i.e., affirming or denying a predicate of a subject without any condition. The commentator explains why it is called hamliyya.
Example: 'Zayd is standing' is categorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ وَالثَّانِي:
قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ القَضَايَا ... إلخ) «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِي فَقَط ، كَمَا قَالَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ قَوْلُهُ : ( شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ) بَدَلَانِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : قِسْمَانِ).
وَالأُوْلَى: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّرْطِ وَالتَّعْلِيقِ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي، وَالثَّانِيَةُ: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الحَمْلِ، وَلِذَلِكَ سُمِّيَتِ الأُوْلَى: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَالثَّانِيَةُ: «حَمْلِيَّةٌ».
الأنبابي
قوله : (وَالأُوْلَى : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا ... إلخ) وسميت «شرطيّة»; لوجود أداة الشَّرط فيها، لفظاً أو تقديراً ؛ ليشمل المنفصلة، فإنَّ قولنا : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، أَوْ فَرْداً في قوَّة قولنا : «إِنْ كَانَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْداً، وَإِنْ كَانَ فَرْداً لَمْ يَكُنْ زَوْجاً».
(من صفحة 238) وَقَدِ اشْتُهِرَ : أَنَّ الأَوْلَى: مَا لَيْسَ طَرَفَاهَا مُفْرَدَيْنِ وَلَا فِي قُوَّتِهِمَا، وَالثَّانِيَةَ: «مَا طَرَفَاهَا مُفْرَدَانِ أَوْ فِي قُوَّتِهِمَا»؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ ، وَكَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ يُنَاقِضُ زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِقَائِم.»؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ: «هَذَا نَقِيضُ هَذَا». وَاعْتُرِضَ بِأَنَّ الأُوْلَى فِي قُوَّةِ المُفْرَدَيْنِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا إِذَا كَانَتْ مُتَّصِلَةٌ تَكُونُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «هَذَا مَلْزُومٌ لِذَاكَ»، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ تَكُونُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «هَذَا مُعَانِدٌ لِذَاكَ». وَأُجِيبَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ بِمَا لَا يَنْهَضُ، فَالأَوْلَى حَذْفُ ذَلِكَ، وَالاقْتِصَارُ عَلَى مَا تَقَدَّمَ، كَمَا يُفِيدُهُ كَلَامُ المَلوِيِّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالثَّانِي) إِنَّمَا قَالَ: وَالثَّانِي، وَلَمْ يَقُلْ: وَالثَّانِيَة مَعَ أَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ الحَمْلِيَّةِ؛ نَظَراً لِكَوْنِهَا قِسْماً، وَسَيَأْتِي الكَلَامُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ فِي قَوْلِهِ: وَإِنْ كَانَ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ ... إلخ .
Then propositions according to them are two types: conditional and categorical. And the second: His statement: (Then propositions... etc.) 'Thumma' is for sequential order only, as al-Mallawi said. His statement: (conditional, categorical) are alternatives to his statement: two types. And the first: 'that in which judgment is made in the manner of condition and dependency,' as will come, and the second: 'that in which judgment is made in the manner of predication,' therefore the first is called conditional and the second categorical. Al-Annababi: His statement: (and the first: that in which judgment is made... etc.) It is called conditional because of the presence of the particle of condition in it, literally or virtually, to include the disjunctive. For our statement: 'Either the number is even or odd' is in the strength of our statement: 'If the number is even, it is not odd, and if it is odd, it is not even.' And it has become well-known that the first is: that whose two extremes are not single terms nor in their strength, and the second: 'that whose two extremes are single terms or in their strength'; like your saying: 'Zayd is standing,' and like your saying: 'Zayd is standing contradicts Zayd is not standing'; because it is in the strength of: 'This is the contradictory of this.' And it was objected that the first is in the strength of single terms; because if it is conjunctive, it is in the strength of saying: 'This is necessarily accompanied by that,' and if it is disjunctive, it is in the strength of saying: 'This is opposite to that.' And it was answered with something that does not suffice, so it is better to delete that and restrict to what preceded, as indicated by al-Mallawi's statement in his large commentary. His statement: (and the second) he said 'al-thani' and not 'al-thaniya' even though it refers to the categorical; considering it as a division, and the discussion of the first will come in his statement: 'and if it is conditional... etc.'
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions into two main types: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). The author begins by stating that propositions are divided into these two. The commentator explains that the word 'thumma' here is only for sequential order in mention, not for ranking. The two types are given as alternatives to the statement 'two types'. The first type is defined as a judgment made in a conditional and dependent manner, while the second is a judgment made in the manner of predication (haml). Therefore, the first is called conditional and the second categorical. Al-Annababi adds that the conditional is called such because it contains a conditional particle explicitly or implicitly, to include disjunctive conditionals. For example, 'Either the number is even or odd' is equivalent to saying 'If the number is even, it is not odd, and if it is odd, it is not even.' Then the passage mentions a well-known alternative definition: conditional is that whose two extremes are not single terms nor in their strength, while categorical is that whose two extremes are single terms or in their strength. Examples are given: 'Zayd is standing' is categorical; 'Zayd is standing contradicts Zayd is not standing' is conditional because it is equivalent to saying 'This is the contradictory of this.' An objection is raised: the conditional is also in the strength of single terms because a conjunctive conditional implies 'this entails that' and a disjunctive implies 'this is opposite to that,' which are relations between single terms. The answer given is weak, so the commentator recommends discarding this alternative definition and sticking with the original definition of judgment on the pattern of condition or predication. Finally, a grammatical note: the author said 'al-thani' (masculine) instead of 'al-thaniya' (feminine) because it is referring to 'qism' (division), not to 'hamliyya' directly.

Key Terms
شرطية
حملیة
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the disjunctive proposition 'Either the number is even or odd' considered conditional?
A) Because it contains a conditional particle explicitly.
B) Because it is equivalent to a conjunctive conditional 'If it is even, then it is not odd...'
C) Because its extremes are single terms.
D) Because it makes a categorical judgment.

Key Takeaway
Propositions are divided into conditional and categorical based on whether the judgment is conditional or predicative; this foundational distinction is essential for all logical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each proposition as conditional or categorical: (a) 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' (b) 'If it is intoxicant, then it is forbidden.' (c) 'Either the thief is punished or he repays.'

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Alternative Definition and Its Rejection
Controversy about the alternative definition using extremes, with objection and weak answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the alternative definition of conditionals (based on extremes) and explain why the commentator recommends discarding it.

Core Idea
A definition that seems intuitive can be problematic; the safer path is to rely on the judgment form.

A well-known alternative definition states that a conditional is a proposition whose extremes are not single terms. While this seems clear, an objection exposes its weakness. The commentator reviews the debate and concludes it's better to delete this definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the alternative definition
Read: 'the first is that whose two extremes are not single terms nor in their strength, and the second is that whose two extremes are single terms or in their strength.' This defines conditionals by the nature of their parts.
Example given: 'Zayd is standing' has single terms; 'Zayd is standing contradicts Zayd is not standing' is equivalent to a contradiction relation between two propositions.
2
Analyze the example provided
The example 'Zayd is standing contradicts Zayd is not standing' is said to be in the strength of 'This is the contradictory of that'. This is meant to illustrate a conditional with single-term extremes? Actually it shows that a conditional can be rephrased as relation between terms.
3
Trace the objection
The objection: the conditional is also in the strength of single terms because a conjunctive implies 'this entails that' and a disjunctive implies 'this is opposite to that' — these are relations between single terms (like 'entailment' and 'opposition'), not propositions.
So the alternative definition fails to distinguish conditionals from categoricals clearly.
4
Assess the response and recommendation
The answer given is weak ('لا ينهض'), so the commentator says it is better to discard this definition entirely and stick with the original one based on the form of judgment.
Al-Mallawi's large commentary is cited as supporting this recommendation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ وَالثَّانِي:
قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ القَضَايَا ... إلخ) «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِي فَقَط ، كَمَا قَالَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ قَوْلُهُ : ( شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ) بَدَلَانِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : قِسْمَانِ).
وَالأُوْلَى: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّرْطِ وَالتَّعْلِيقِ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي، وَالثَّانِيَةُ: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الحَمْلِ، وَلِذَلِكَ سُمِّيَتِ الأُوْلَى: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَالثَّانِيَةُ: «حَمْلِيَّةٌ».
الأنبابي
قوله : (وَالأُوْلَى : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا ... إلخ) وسميت «شرطيّة»; لوجود أداة الشَّرط فيها، لفظاً أو تقديراً ؛ ليشمل المنفصلة، فإنَّ قولنا : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً، أَوْ فَرْداً في قوَّة قولنا : «إِنْ كَانَ العَدَدُ زَوْجاً لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْداً، وَإِنْ كَانَ فَرْداً لَمْ يَكُنْ زَوْجاً».
(من صفحة 238) وَقَدِ اشْتُهِرَ : أَنَّ الأَوْلَى: مَا لَيْسَ طَرَفَاهَا مُفْرَدَيْنِ وَلَا فِي قُوَّتِهِمَا، وَالثَّانِيَةَ: «مَا طَرَفَاهَا مُفْرَدَانِ أَوْ فِي قُوَّتِهِمَا»؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ ، وَكَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ يُنَاقِضُ زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِقَائِم.»؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ: «هَذَا نَقِيضُ هَذَا». وَاعْتُرِضَ بِأَنَّ الأُوْلَى فِي قُوَّةِ المُفْرَدَيْنِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا إِذَا كَانَتْ مُتَّصِلَةٌ تَكُونُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «هَذَا مَلْزُومٌ لِذَاكَ»، وَإِذَا كَانَتْ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ تَكُونُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «هَذَا مُعَانِدٌ لِذَاكَ». وَأُجِيبَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ بِمَا لَا يَنْهَضُ، فَالأَوْلَى حَذْفُ ذَلِكَ، وَالاقْتِصَارُ عَلَى مَا تَقَدَّمَ، كَمَا يُفِيدُهُ كَلَامُ المَلوِيِّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالثَّانِي) إِنَّمَا قَالَ: وَالثَّانِي، وَلَمْ يَقُلْ: وَالثَّانِيَة مَعَ أَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ الحَمْلِيَّةِ؛ نَظَراً لِكَوْنِهَا قِسْماً، وَسَيَأْتِي الكَلَامُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ فِي قَوْلِهِ: وَإِنْ كَانَ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ ... إلخ .
Then propositions according to them are two types: conditional and categorical. And the second: His statement: (Then propositions... etc.) 'Thumma' is for sequential order only, as al-Mallawi said. His statement: (conditional, categorical) are alternatives to his statement: two types. And the first: 'that in which judgment is made in the manner of condition and dependency,' as will come, and the second: 'that in which judgment is made in the manner of predication,' therefore the first is called conditional and the second categorical. Al-Annababi: His statement: (and the first: that in which judgment is made... etc.) It is called conditional because of the presence of the particle of condition in it, literally or virtually, to include the disjunctive. For our statement: 'Either the number is even or odd' is in the strength of our statement: 'If the number is even, it is not odd, and if it is odd, it is not even.' And it has become well-known that the first is: that whose two extremes are not single terms nor in their strength, and the second: 'that whose two extremes are single terms or in their strength'; like your saying: 'Zayd is standing,' and like your saying: 'Zayd is standing contradicts Zayd is not standing'; because it is in the strength of: 'This is the contradictory of this.' And it was objected that the first is in the strength of single terms; because if it is conjunctive, it is in the strength of saying: 'This is necessarily accompanied by that,' and if it is disjunctive, it is in the strength of saying: 'This is opposite to that.' And it was answered with something that does not suffice, so it is better to delete that and restrict to what preceded, as indicated by al-Mallawi's statement in his large commentary. His statement: (and the second) he said 'al-thani' and not 'al-thaniya' even though it refers to the categorical; considering it as a division, and the discussion of the first will come in his statement: 'and if it is conditional... etc.'
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions into two main types: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). The author begins by stating that propositions are divided into these two. The commentator explains that the word 'thumma' here is only for sequential order in mention, not for ranking. The two types are given as alternatives to the statement 'two types'. The first type is defined as a judgment made in a conditional and dependent manner, while the second is a judgment made in the manner of predication (haml). Therefore, the first is called conditional and the second categorical. Al-Annababi adds that the conditional is called such because it contains a conditional particle explicitly or implicitly, to include disjunctive conditionals. For example, 'Either the number is even or odd' is equivalent to saying 'If the number is even, it is not odd, and if it is odd, it is not even.' Then the passage mentions a well-known alternative definition: conditional is that whose two extremes are not single terms nor in their strength, while categorical is that whose two extremes are single terms or in their strength. Examples are given: 'Zayd is standing' is categorical; 'Zayd is standing contradicts Zayd is not standing' is conditional because it is equivalent to saying 'This is the contradictory of this.' An objection is raised: the conditional is also in the strength of single terms because a conjunctive conditional implies 'this entails that' and a disjunctive implies 'this is opposite to that,' which are relations between single terms. The answer given is weak, so the commentator recommends discarding this alternative definition and sticking with the original definition of judgment on the pattern of condition or predication. Finally, a grammatical note: the author said 'al-thani' (masculine) instead of 'al-thaniya' (feminine) because it is referring to 'qism' (division), not to 'hamliyya' directly.

قوة

Why does the commentator recommend discarding the alternative definition?
A) Because it is not well-known.

C) Because the objection was not sufficiently answered.

Key Takeaway
When a definition faces a strong objection that cannot be adequately answered, it is better to revert to a more robust definition.

?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions
Author defines three types of disjunctive and their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of disjunctive propositions (mani' al-jam', mani' al-khuluw, mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluw) and explain their logical relationships.

Core Idea
Only the 'preventer of both' is the true exclusive disjunction; the others are partial.

In logic, not all 'either-or' statements are the same. This passage from Bajuri's commentary on Bannani unpacks the precise definitions of three kinds of disjunctive propositions. By understanding the difference between preventing conjunction (both true) and preventing voidness (both false), you will be able to accurately analyze compound statements in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the Arabic and translation to find the definitions of Mani' al-Jam', Mani' al-Khuluw, and Mani' al-Jam' wa al-Khuluw. Note the Arabic terms.
2
Distinguish the definitions of Mani' al-Khuluw
Notice there are two opinions: one where it prevents both truth and falsehood (if parts are contradictories or equivalents), and one where it prevents only falsehood (if one part is more general than the contradictory of the other). The text gives the example 'This is either not-white or not-black'.
The first opinion is the 'well-known' view.
3
Understand Mani' al-Jam' wa al-Khuluw
This is the true exclusive disjunction: it prevents both conjunction (both true) and voidness (both false). It requires a thing and its contradictory or equivalent. The author calls it 'al-haqiqi al-akhass' (the real and most specific).
Compare with the other two: this one has complete mutual exclusion.
4
Map the relationships between the types
Follow the author's analysis: on the first definitions, Mani' al-Jam' and Mani' al-Khuluw intersect in contradictory cases but each has exclusive cases (specific vs general). Mani' al-Jam' wa al-Khuluw is absolutely specific relative to each. On the second definitions, all three are completely disparate.
The author ends with 'فليتأمل' (let it be pondered), indicating careful thought is needed.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the statement: 'The act is either obligatory or impermissible.' Identify which type of disjunction it represents. Check if both parts can be true (no) or both false (yes, if it is mubah). So it is a preventer of conjunction only, not both.
This example shows why precision matters in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ خُلُو) أَيْ: أَوْ مَانِعُ خُلُو . وَاخْتُلِفَ أَيْضاً فِي تَفْسِيرِ مَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ : - فَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ :- : هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزأين كَذِباً وَصِدْقاً ، أَوْ كَذِباً فَالأَوَّلُ : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَالثَّانِي : إِذَا كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَعَمِّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : هَذَا : إِمَّا غَيْرُ أَبْيْضَ، أَوْ غَيْرُ أَسْوَدَ (۱) ، فَإِنَّ غَيْرَ أَسْوَدَ أَعَمُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِ غَيْرِ أَبْيَضَ وَهُوَ أَبْيَضُ؛ لِكَوْنِهِ فَرْداً مِنْهُ. - وَقِيلَ : هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزْاينِ كَذِباً فَقَط» ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأعَمِّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْرُ مَا ذُكِرَ . قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ هُمَا أَيْ: أَوْ مَانِعُهُمَا ، فَالضَّمِيرُ فِي الأَصْلِ مُضَافٌ إِلَيْهِ، فَلَمَّا حُذِف المُضَافُ انْفَصَلَ الضَّمِيرُ وَقَامَ مَقَامَ المُضَافِ. وَمَا نِعَتُهُمَا هِيَ : مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الجُزأينِ صِدْقاً وَكَذِباً ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْء وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ؛ نَحْوُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَهُوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ أَيْ: لِأَنَّ التَّنَافِي فِيهِ أَتَمُّ مِنْهُ فِي الآخَرَيْنِ، فَإِنَّهُ فِيهِ مِنْ جَانِبَي الصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ، بِخِلَافِهِ فِيهِمَا. وَقَوْلُهُ : (الأَخَصُّ) أَيْ: مِنْ مَانِعِ الجَمْعِ وَمِنْ مَانِعِ الخُلُو. فَالنِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ مَانِعَتِهِمَا وَمَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ : - لاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَو المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضه. - وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ. وَكَذَلِكَ النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ مَانِعَتِهِمَا وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ : - لاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ. - وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالْأَعَمِّ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ. وَأَمَّا النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ فَالعُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ : - لِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ، أَوِ المُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضه. - وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالْأَخَصُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ. - وَانْفِرَادِ مَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ فِي المُرَكَّبَةِ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالأَعَمُ مِنْ نَقِيضِهِ. هَذَا كُلُّهُ عَلَى القَوْلِ الأَوَّلِ فِي كُلِّ مِنْ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى القَوْلِ الثَّانِي فِي ذَلِكَ فَالنِّسْبَةُ فِي ذَلِكَ كُلِّهِ التَّبَايُنُ ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
His statement: (or khulu) i.e., or a preventer of voidness. And there is disagreement also in the interpretation of the proposition preventing voidness: - It was said - and it is the well-known -: it is that in which mutual exclusion is judged between the two parts in falsehood and truth, or in falsehood only. The first: when it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or what is equivalent to its contradictory; like what preceded. The second: when it is composed of a thing and the more general than its contradictory; like your saying: This is either not-white or not-black, for not-black is more general than the contradictory of not-white which is white, because it is a particular of it. - And it was said: it is that in which mutual exclusion is judged between the two parts in falsehood only; such that it is composed of a thing and the more general than its contradictory; like what was mentioned. His statement: (or both) i.e., or the preventer of both, so the pronoun originally was attached to it (the addressee), but when the attached noun was deleted, the pronoun became separate and took the place of the attached noun. And the preventer of both is that in which mutual exclusion is judged between the two parts in truth and falsehood; such that it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or what is equivalent to its contradictory; like what preceded. His statement: (and it is the real) i.e., because the mutual exclusion in it is more complete than in the other two, for in it it is from both sides of truth and falsehood, contrary to them. And his statement: (the most specific) i.e., than the preventer of conjunction and than the preventer of voidness. So the relationship between the preventer of both and the preventer of conjunction is general and specific absolute: - because they meet in the proposition composed of a thing and its contradictory, or what is equivalent to its contradictory. - and the exclusivity of the preventer of conjunction in the proposition composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory. Likewise the relationship between the preventer of both and the preventer of voidness: - because they meet in the proposition composed of a thing and its contradictory, or what is equivalent to its contradictory. - and the exclusivity of the preventer of voidness in the proposition composed of a thing and the more general than its contradictory. As for the relationship between the preventer of conjunction and the preventer of voidness, it is general and specific from a certain perspective: - because they meet in the proposition composed of a thing and its contradictory, or what is equivalent to its contradictory. - and the exclusivity of the preventer of conjunction in the proposition composed of a thing and the more specific than its contradictory. - and the exclusivity of the preventer of voidness in the proposition composed of a thing and the more general than its contradictory. All this is according to the first opinion in each of the preventer of conjunction and the preventer of voidness. As for according to the second opinion in that, the relationship in all of that is complete disparity, so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage expands on the types of disjunctive propositions, specifically the three forms: preventer of conjunction (mani' al-jam'), preventer of voidness (mani' al-khuluw), and the one that prevents both (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluw), which is called the true and most specific. The author first discusses the disagreement in defining the preventer of voidness. According to the well-known view, it is a proposition where the two parts cannot both be false and cannot both be true, or cannot both be false only. The first case occurs when the proposition is composed of a thing and its contradictory, or what is equivalent to its contradictory—for example, 'This is either white or not-white'. The second case occurs when it is composed of a thing and something more general than its contradictory, like 'This is either not-white or not-black'. Here, 'not-black' is more general than the contradictory of 'not-white', which is 'white', because 'white' is a specific instance of 'not-black'. Another opinion restricts the preventer of voidness to only preventing both being false, i.e., they cannot both be false, but they can both be true. This occurs only when one part is more general than the contradictory of the other. The author then explains the 'preventer of both' (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluw) which is a proposition that prevents both conjunction and disjunction—i.e., the two parts cannot both be true and cannot both be false. This happens when it is composed of a thing and its contradictory or its equivalent. This is the 'true' disjunctive (haqiqi) because the mutual exclusion is complete, on both sides of truth and falsehood, unlike the other two. It is also the most specific (akhass) because it applies to fewer cases. Next, the author compares the relationships between these three types. On the first definition of each, the relationship between the preventer of both and the preventer of conjunction is absolute generality and specificity: they coincide in cases where the parts are contradictories or equivalents, but the preventer of conjunction alone also applies when one part is more specific than the contradictory of the other. Similarly, the relationship between the preventer of both and the preventer of voidness is also absolute generality and specificity: they coincide in contradictory cases, but the preventer of voidness alone applies when one part is more general than the contradictory of the other. The relationship between the preventer of conjunction and the preventer of voidness is general and specific from a certain perspective: they coincide in contradictory cases, but each has exclusive cases: the preventer of conjunction applies when one part is more specific than the contradictory, while the preventer of voidness applies when one part is more general than the contradictory. Finally, the author notes that if one adopts the second definition (where the preventer of voidness only prevents falsehood, not truth), then all three are completely disparate (tabayun), meaning they have no overlap. This is left for the reader to ponder.

Key Terms
مانع الجمع
مانع الخلو
مانعهما
الحقيقي الأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a 'preventer of voidness' (mani' al-khuluw) according to the first opinion mentioned?
A) This is either white or not-white.
B) This is either not-white or not-black.
C) This is either white or black.
D) This is either a human or a stone.

Key Takeaway
The true exclusive disjunction must prevent all shared truth and shared falsehood; the other types are weaker and apply in specific contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which type of disjunction is present in the statement: 'The world is either created or eternal.' Explain why.
?
Given the two opinions on Mani' al-Khuluw, determine whether the statement 'This is either not-white or not-black' can be used in a valid argument if both parts could be true. What is the logical issue?

The Definition of Qiyas and Essential Entailment
Definition of qiyas with condition of essential entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a compound of premises satisfies the condition of essential entailment (istilzam bi al-dhat) required for a valid qiyas.

Core Idea
A syllogism's conclusion must follow from the premises alone, without external principles.

In this passage, al-Bajuri unpacks the definition of qiyas (syllogism) given in the matn. The first key condition is that the entailment must be 'by essence' (bi al-dhat), meaning the conclusion necessarily results from the premises without needing an additional premise. Examples of equality and disparity chains illustrate why this condition matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic: 'القياس' (qiyas). The definition begins with 'إن القياس من قضايا...'
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Genus: 'من قضايا' (a compound of propositions). Differentia: 'مستلزما بالذات قولا آخرا' (essentially entails another statement). The differentia has two parts: 'بالذات' (essentially) and 'قولا آخرا' (a single new statement). Focus here on 'بالذات'.
3
Understand the condition of essential entailment
Essential entailment means the conclusion must follow from the premises themselves, without relying on an external principle. Al-Bajuri gives two examples: (1) Equality chain: 'Zayd equals Amr, Amr equals Bakr' entails 'Zayd equals Bakr' only because of the external principle 'equals of equals are equal', so it is not a valid qiyas. (2) Disparity chain: 'Human is disparate from horse, horse is disparate from rational' does not entail 'human is disparate from rational', so no qiyas.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Apply the condition. For example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, wine is an intoxicant, therefore wine is forbidden' – the conclusion follows from the premises alone, valid. But 'Ali is the father of Hasan, Hasan is the father of Husayn, therefore Ali is the grandfather of Husayn' relies on the external principle of transitivity, so not a valid qiyas.

Source Text

باب في القياس
(۷۳) إِنَّ القِيَاسَ مِنْ قَضَايَا صُوِّرًا مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَا
الباجوري
مَحْمُولِ أُوْلَاهُمَا مَوْضُوعُ أُخْرَاهُمَا ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مِنْ مَادَّةِ المُسَاوَاةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «زَيْدٌ مُسَاوِ لِعَمْرِو ، وَعَمْرُو مُسَاءٍ لِبَكْرِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ وَإِنِ اسْتَلْزَمَ قَوْلاً آخَرَ وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ مُسَاءٍ لِبَكْرِ، لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِمُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ وَهِيَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : مُسَاوِي المُسَاوِي لِشَيْءٍ مُسَاوِ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلُّفِ ذَلِكَ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : الإِنْسَانُ مُبَايِنُ لِلفَرَسِ، وَالفَرَسُ مُبَايِنُ لِلنَّاطِقِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ : أَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ مُبَاينٌ لِلنَّاطِقِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقَ أَنْ يُقَالَ (۱) : «مُبَايِنُ المُبَايِنِ لِشَيْءٍ مُبَايِنٌ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء».
- وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا : قَوْلاً آخَرَ» ؛ لِيَخْرُجَ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جِسْمُ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ
لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلاً آخَرَ، وَإِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ اسْتِلْزَامَ الكُلِّ لِجُزْئِهِ (۳).
لَا يُقَالُ: التَّعْرِيفُ شَامِلٌ لِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِعَكْسِهِ ، فَيَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ قَوْلاً آخَرَ . لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : قَدِ اعْتَبَرُوا فِي اللَّازِمِ الإِفْرَادَ؛ حَيْثُ قَالُوا : قَوْلاً آخَرَ، فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ قَوْلاً وَاحِداً، وَذَلِكَ يَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلَيْنِ لَا قَوْلاً وَاحِداً ؛ لأَنَّ كُلَّ قَضِيَّةٍ مِنْهُ تَسْتَلْزِمُ عَكْسَهَا . وَأَورَدَ : أَنَّهُ (۳) إِذَا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، أَنْتَجَ عَيْنَ الصُّغْرَى، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ أَنْتَجَ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى، فَكُلُّ مِنْ هَذَيْنِ القِيَاسَيْنِ لَمْ يَسْتَلْزِمْ قَوْلاً آخَرَ، وَإِنَّمَا اسْتَلْزَمَ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ .
وَأُجِيبَ بِأَوْجُهِ المُتَّجِهُ مِنْهَا : مَنَعُ أَنَّ نَحْوَ ذَلِكَ يُسَمَّى قِيَاساً ؛ لِفَسَادِ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ بِاتِّحَادِ
طَرَفَيْهَا ، فَلَا تَغْفَلْ.
Section on Qiyas. (73) 'Qiyas is a compound of propositions, such that it essentially entails another statement.' Al-Bajuri: ... (with examples and objections as in Arabic).
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) and explains its conditions. The definition: a syllogism is a composition of premises that necessarily produces another statement (conclusion) by itself. The first condition is essential entailment (bi al-dhat), excluding cases where entailment relies on external premises, e.g., equality chains (Zayd equals Amr, Amr equals Bakr entails Zayd equals Bakr only because of the principle that equals of equals are equal). Similarly, disparity chains fail to entail. The second condition is that the entailment must be a single new statement, excluding cases where no new statement results (e.g., 'Every human is an animal; every stone is a body' yields nothing new) or where multiple statements result (e.g., from conversion). The objection about tautological syllogisms (e.g., 'Every human is human; every human is animal') is answered by deeming such syllogisms invalid due to the defect of having identical terms in a premise.

Key Terms
القياس
استلزام بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following compounds does NOT satisfy the condition of essential entailment?
A) Every A is B, every B is C, therefore every A is C.
B) A is the same age as B, B is the same age as C, therefore A is the same age as C.
C) Every human is mortal, every philosopher is human, therefore every philosopher is mortal.
D) No A is B, some B is C, therefore some C is not A.

Key Takeaway
The condition of essential entailment ensures that the conclusion is a direct consequence of the premises, not dependent on an external principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following compound: 'Zayd is the father of Amr, Amr is the father of Bakr, therefore Zayd is the grandfather of Bakr.' Does it satisfy the condition of essential entailment? Explain your reasoning.
?
Construct a valid qiyas on an Islamic topic (e.g., from fiqh or kalam) that does satisfy essential entailment, and explain why it does.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Condition of Producing a Single New Statement
Explanation of the condition of producing a single new statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a syllogism meets the condition of producing a single new statement (qawlan akhara), excluding tautologies and multiple conclusions.

Core Idea
A valid qiyas must yield exactly one new proposition, not a restatement or multiple conclusions.

The definition of qiyas includes the phrase 'qawlan akhara' (another statement). Al-Bajuri explains that this condition excludes two types of invalid compounds: those that produce no new statement (e.g., unrelated premises) and those that produce multiple statements (e.g., two conclusions from conversion). He also addresses a special objection about tautological premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Look for the phrase 'قولا آخرا' in the definition. It means the syllogism must entail exactly one new statement that is not one of the premises.
2
Understand exclusion of no new statement
Al-Bajuri gives example: 'Every human is an animal, every stone is a body' – these premises have no middle term, so they entail no new statement; they only entail one of the premises (like the whole entails its part). This is not a qiyas.
3
Understand exclusion of multiple statements
An objector notes that if a premise entails its converse (e.g., from 'Every A is B' we get 'Some B is A'), then a syllogism with that premise might entail two statements. Al-Bajuri argues that the definition requires a single new statement; if multiple are entailed, it is not a qiyas.
4
Analyze the tautology objection
Another objection: syllogisms like 'Every human is human, every human is animal' yield the major premise as conclusion (no new statement). Al-Bajuri responds that such syllogisms are invalid because one premise is defective (both terms identical), so they are not considered qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۷۳) إِنَّ القِيَاسَ مِنْ قَضَايَا صُوِّرًا مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَا
الباجوري
مَحْمُولِ أُوْلَاهُمَا مَوْضُوعُ أُخْرَاهُمَا ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مِنْ مَادَّةِ المُسَاوَاةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «زَيْدٌ مُسَاوِ لِعَمْرِو ، وَعَمْرُو مُسَاءٍ لِبَكْرِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ وَإِنِ اسْتَلْزَمَ قَوْلاً آخَرَ وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ مُسَاءٍ لِبَكْرِ، لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِمُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ وَهِيَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : مُسَاوِي المُسَاوِي لِشَيْءٍ مُسَاوِ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء؛ بِدَلِيلِ تَخَلُّفِ ذَلِكَ فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ : الإِنْسَانُ مُبَايِنُ لِلفَرَسِ، وَالفَرَسُ مُبَايِنُ لِلنَّاطِقِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ : أَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ مُبَاينٌ لِلنَّاطِقِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقَ أَنْ يُقَالَ (۱) : «مُبَايِنُ المُبَايِنِ لِشَيْءٍ مُبَايِنٌ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء».
- وَإِنَّمَا قُلْنَا : قَوْلاً آخَرَ» ؛ لِيَخْرُجَ نَحْرُ قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جِسْمُ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ

لَا يُقَالُ: التَّعْرِيفُ شَامِلٌ لِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِعَكْسِهِ ، فَيَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ قَوْلاً آخَرَ . لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : قَدِ اعْتَبَرُوا فِي اللَّازِمِ الإِفْرَادَ؛ حَيْثُ قَالُوا : قَوْلاً آخَرَ، فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ قَوْلاً وَاحِداً، وَذَلِكَ يَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلَيْنِ لَا قَوْلاً وَاحِداً ؛ لأَنَّ كُلَّ قَضِيَّةٍ مِنْهُ تَسْتَلْزِمُ عَكْسَهَا . وَأَورَدَ : أَنَّهُ (۳) إِذَا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، أَنْتَجَ عَيْنَ الصُّغْرَى، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ أَنْتَجَ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى، فَكُلُّ مِنْ هَذَيْنِ القِيَاسَيْنِ لَمْ يَسْتَلْزِمْ قَوْلاً آخَرَ، وَإِنَّمَا اسْتَلْزَمَ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ .

According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a valid qiyas?
A) Every human is animal; every animal is mortal; therefore every human is mortal.
B) Every human is human; every human is animal; therefore every human is animal.

The Syllogism

Simple vs. Compound Syllogisms
Distinguishing simple and compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a simple syllogism from a compound syllogism and explain how a compound syllogism reduces to multiple simple syllogisms.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism is a chain of simple syllogisms, not a single argument.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary distinguishes syllogisms by the number of premises: simple (two premises) and compound (three or more). The deeper point is that a multi-premise syllogism is actually a series of simple arguments linked together. Understanding this reduction is crucial for analyzing complex proofs in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find in the text the definition of simple and compound syllogisms. Note the examples: the simple one is 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated' (two premises), and the compound one is 'The nabbaas takes property secretly, every secret taker is a thief, every thief is to have his hand cut off' (three premises).
The simple syllogism has exactly two premises, the compound has three or more.
2
Recognize the reduction
See how the compound example is analyzed as two simple syllogisms. The first: 'nabbaas takes secretly' and 'every secret taker is a thief' yields 'nabbaas is a thief'. The second: 'nabbaas is a thief' and 'every thief has his hand cut off' yields 'nabbaas has his hand cut off'. The conclusion of the first becomes a premise of the second.
3
Understand the author's wording
Notice why the author used 'qadaya' (premises) instead of 'muqaddimat' (propositions). Using 'muqaddimat' would cause circularity, because a muqaddima is defined as part of a qiyas. Also note the emphatic particle 'inna' to clarify the meaning of qiyas (logical vs. usuli).
4
Apply the distinction
Given any argument with multiple premises, determine if it is a single simple syllogism or a compound of several. Practice with the fiqh example provided in the 'where_this_tool_lives' section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِنَّ القِيَاسَ ... إلخ) إِنَّمَا أَتَى بِـ «إِنَّ المُفِيدَةِ لِلتَّوكِيدِ ؛ لِأَنَّ المَقَامَ مَقَامُ أَنْ يَتَرَدَّدَ فِي مَعْنَى القِيَاسِ هَلْ هُوَ المَعْنَى الأُصُولِيّ أَوِ المَنْطِقِي؟ (۱) وَحِينَئِذٍ فَالمُخَاطَبُ إِمَّا مُتَرَدِّدٌ فِي ذَلِكَ، أَوْ مُنَزَّلٌ مَنْزِلَةَ المُتَرَدِّدِ ، فَحَسُنَ التَّوْكِيدُ بـ «إِنَّ ) .
قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ قَضَايَا مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِمَا بَعْدَهُ، وَالمُرَادُ بِالجَمْعِ اثْنَتَانِ فَأَكْثَرَ ، فَشَمَلَ :
- المُرَكَّبَ مِنْ قَضِيّتَيْنِ ؛ نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرِ حَادِثٌ .
- وَالمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ أَكْثَرَ؛ نَحْوُ قَوْلِكَ : «النَّبَّاسُ آخِذُ لِلمَالِ خُفْيَةً، وَكُلُّ آخِذِ لِلمَالِ خُفْيَةً سَارِقٌ، وَكُلُّ سَارِقٍ تُقْطَعُ يَدُهُ». (۲)
وَالأَوَّلُ يُسَمَّى : بسيطاً»، وَالثَّانِي : مُرَكَّباً» (٢) هَذَا عَلَى رَأي مَنْ يَقُولُ بـ: «أَنَّ القِيَاسَ المُرَكَّبَ قِيَاسُ وَاحِدٌ، وَالصَّحِيحُ عِنْدَ المُحَقِّقِينَ : أَنَّهُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى أَفْيسَةٍ بسيطةٍ (۳) ، كَمَا سَيَأْتِي بَيَانُهُ.
وَعَلَيْهِ فَيُجَابُ عَمَّنْ ذَكَرَ الجَمْعَ كَالمُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّهُ أَطْلَقَ الجَمْعَ وَأَرَادَ المُثَنَّى ، وَكَثِيراً مَا يُسْتَعْمَلُ ذَلِكَ، أَوْ أَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِهِ اثْنَيْنِ فَأَكْثَرَ؛ نَظَراً إِلَى صُورَةِ التَّرْكِيبِ ظَاهِراً . وَإِنَّمَا قَالَ المُصَنِّفُ : مِنْ قَضَايَا، وَلَمْ يَقُلْ : مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ»؛ لِئَلَّا يَلْزَمَ الدَّورُ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُمْ
الأنبابي
قوله : (هَلْ هُوَ المَعْنَى الأُصُولِ أَوِ المَنْطِقِي؟) فيه : أنَّ المخاطب بهذا التّعريف جاهل بالقياس المنطقي، والمقصودُ مِنَ التَّعريف بيان المعنى وكشفه، لا التَّعيين؛ على أنَّ المقام يدفع هذا التَّرَدُّد. قوله : (وَالمُرَادُ بِالجَمْعِ... إلخ) قال بعض مشايخنا : (مِنْ) تبعيضيَّةٌ، فلا حاجة إلى تأويل القضايا بما فوق الواحد.
قوله : (النَّاسُ) أي: للقبور أو ما هو أعم .
قوله : (وَالصَّحِيحُ ... إلخ) حاصله : أنَّه تؤخذ نتيجة القياس الأوّل، وتجعل صغرى القياس الثاني
وهكذا .
(۱) الشرشيمي : قوله : (أَوِ المَنْطِقِي) أي : أوِ اللُّغوي.
(۲) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( وَالثَّاني : مُرَكَّباً) أي: من ثلاث مقدمات .
(۳) الشرشيمي : قوله : (يَرْجِعُ إِلَى أَقْسَةٍ بَسِيطَةٍ) أي : قياسين من أربعة قضايا ؛ إلَّا أنَّه حذف صغرى القياس الثاني، وهي نتيجة
القياس الأول.
His statement: (Indeed, qiyas... etc.) He used 'indeed' for emphasis because the context is that one might be uncertain about the meaning of qiyas: is it the usuli meaning or the logical? And so the addressee is either uncertain or considered as such, so emphasis with 'indeed' is appropriate. His statement: (from premises) is attached to what follows, and by the plural is meant two or more, so it includes: the compound of two statements, like your saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated'; and the compound of more, like your saying: 'The nabbaas is a taker of property secretly, and every taker of property secretly is a thief, and every thief is to have his hand cut off.' The first is called 'simple', and the second 'compound'. This is according to the view that the compound syllogism is one syllogism; but the correct view according to the verifiers is that it reduces to simple syllogisms, as will be explained. Accordingly, one responds to the one who mentioned the plural like the author: that he used the plural intending the dual, and often this is used, or that he meant two or more, looking at the form of the composition outwardly. And the author said 'from premises' and did not say 'from propositions' to avoid circularity, because they... (marginal notes follow).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage explains two aspects: first, why the author used the emphatic particle 'inna' in the definition – because the hearer might confuse the logical qiyas with the usuli qiyas (analogy). Second, it clarifies 'min qadaya' (from premises) meaning two or more. Examples: simple syllogism (two premises) like 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated' and compound syllogism (three premises) like the example of the grave-robber (al-nabbaas): 'He takes property secretly, every secret taker is a thief, every thief is to have his hand cut off.' The passage then discusses the debate: some consider the compound as one syllogism, but the correct view (according to the muhaqqiqun) is that it reduces to multiple simple syllogisms (the conclusion of the first becomes a premise of the second). Finally, it explains why the author used 'qadaya' instead of 'muqaddimat' to avoid circularity (since a muqaddima is defined as part of a qiyas).

القياس البسيط
القياس المركب
المقدمة

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Any syllogism with more than two premises is actually a chain of simple two-premise syllogisms, each linked by the conclusion of the preceding one.

Resolving the Temporal Paradox of Entailment
Author addresses an objection that entailment must be after formation, not simultaneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the grammatical state (hal) construction resolves the apparent temporal conflict between syllogism formation and entailment.

Core Idea
Entailment is not temporal but logical, concurrent by the thing's nature.

When the logician says the syllogism is 'mustalziman' (entailing) for the conclusion, the word is in the state (hal) form, implying simultaneity. But an objector argues that entailment only occurs after the syllogism is fully formed. How can it be simultaneous? The response taps into Arabic grammar: the state indicates approximation (muqarana), not strict temporal co-occurrence, and what is 'approximate' depends on the nature of the thing. This lesson unpacks that resolution and shows how it preserves the logical relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical issue
The word 'mustalziman' is a hal (state) from the pronoun in 'suri' (formed). In Arabic grammar, hal denotes a simultaneous circumstance. Why would the author choose this construction?
Hal typically indicates contemporaneity, but the objector takes it as strict time.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says: The syllogism entails the conclusion only after (ʿaqibahu) it is formed, not during its formation. Thus the hal suggests a false simultaneity.
This is a linguistic-logical objection from apparent temporal order.
3
Analyze the response: hal as approximation
The response grants the assumption but says: hal indicates muqarana (approximation), which is relative to each thing. For a syllogism, 'while forming' means 'by virtue of its formation', not necessarily at the same clock tick.
Muqarana is a logical concomitance, not temporal concurrence.
4
Distinguish clear vs. non-clear entailment
The passage then says 'istilzam' here includes both bayyin (clear, no conversion needed) and ghayr bayyin (needs conversion). Complete syllogisms (first figure) entail clearly; incomplete ones entail mediately through transformation.
This shows that 'mustalziman' covers both immediate and mediated entailment.
5
Apply to usul al-fiqh example
Consider a syllogistic proof in usul: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' The entailment is not temporal; it is a logical relation that holds once the premises are accepted. The hal construction signals that the conclusion is 'entailed' by the very structure of the argument, not after a pause.
The logical entailment is simultaneous in the mind, even if verbally sequential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (مُسْتَلْزِماً) حَالٌ مِنَ الضَّمِيرِ فِي قَوْلِهِ : صُورًا. 
وَاعْتُرِضَ : بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ اسْتِلْزَامَ القِيَاسِ لِلنَّتِيجَةِ مَعَ التَّصْوِيرِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُهَا عَقِبَهُ .
وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ عَلَى تَسْلِيمٍ (٢) ذَلِكَ نَقُولُ : الحَالِيَّةُ تَدُلُّ عَلَى المُقَارَنَةِ، وَهِيَ فِي كُلِّ شَيْءٍ بِحَسَبِهِ، كَمَا هُوَ شَائِعٌ . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ : مَا يَعُمُّ البَيِّنَ وَغَيْرَهُ، فَشَمَلَ كَلَامُهُ :
- المُرَكَّبَ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ: «القِيَاسِ الكَامِلِ».
- وَالمُرَكَّبَ مِنْ سَائِرِ (۳) الأَشْكَالِ، وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ: «غَيْرِ الكَامِلِ».
ثُمَّ قَوْلُهُ : (بِالذَّاتِ) أَيْ : بِذَاتِهِ ، فَـ «أَلْ» عِوَضٌ عَنِ الضَّمِيرِ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ المُجِيزِ لِذَلِكَ.

الأنبابي
قوله : (البَيِّنَ) أي: ما لم يفتقر إلى واسطة، وغيره ما افتقر إليها كتغيير كلِّ مِنَ المقدمتين، أو إحداهما ليرجع القياس إلى الشكل الأول.
(۲) الشرشيمي : قوله : ( عَلَى تَسْلِيم ) أي : فلنا منع ذلك، ونقول : إنَّ معناه مستلزم بعد التصوير .
(۳) في بعض النسخ الخطية: «باقي بدلاً من سائر».
His statement: 'mustalziman' is a state from the pronoun in his statement 'suri'. And it was objected: that this implies that the entailment of the syllogism of the conclusion is with the formation, but it is not so; because it only entails it after it. And it was answered: that assuming that, we say: the state indicates approximation, and it is in every thing according to its nature, as is common. And it is not hidden that what is meant by 'entailment' is what is general to the clear and other than it, so his statement includes:
- The compound from the first figure, which is called 'the complete syllogism'.
- And the compound from the remaining figures, which is called 'the incomplete syllogism'.
Then his statement: 'bidhdhat' means by its essence, so 'al' is a substitute for the pronoun according to the view of those who permit that.

Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'al-bayyin' means what does not need a medium, and other than it is what needs it, like changing each of the two premises or one of them to return the syllogism to the first figure.
(2) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: 'on the assumption' means we can deny that, and we say: its meaning is entailing after the formation.
(3) In some manuscript copies: 'baqi' instead of 'sa'ir'.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage parses the word 'mustalziman' (entailing) in the definition. The state (hal) construction suggests that the syllogism entails the conclusion at the time of its formation. An objection is raised: entailment occurs after the syllogism is formed, not simultaneously. The response explains that the state indicates approximation (muqarana) which is relative—the entailment is concurrent in the sense of the nature of the thing, not necessarily temporal. The commentator then notes that 'entailment' here includes both immediate (clear) and mediated (non-clear) inference, covering complete syllogisms (first figure) and incomplete ones (other figures that need conversion). The phrase 'bidhdhat' (by essence) is also explained as meaning 'by itself', with 'al' standing in for the possessive pronoun. Al-Anbabi clarifies that 'bayyin' (clear) is what does not need a middle step, while other entailments require converting premises to the first figure. The marginal note by al-Shirshimi adds that one could deny the objection entirely and interpret the meaning as 'entailing after formation'.

حَال
اسْتِلْزَام

قِيَاس غَيْر كَامِل

Key Takeaway
The word 'mustalziman' in the definition of qiyas is a grammatical state that indicates logical concomitance, not temporal simultaneity, and it covers both immediate and mediated entailment.
