Defining 'Another Statement': The Conclusion's Difference from Premises
Author addresses objection that the conclusion shares parts with premises, so not truly 'other'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what 'mughayara' means in the context of the syllogism's conclusion and defend the definition against the objection that the conclusion shares terms with the premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion is new even when its parts are old.

The definition of syllogism requires that it entail 'another statement' (qawlan akhara). But an objection arises: the conclusion is composed of terms from the premises, so how can it be truly 'other'? In this passage, the author clarifies the precise meaning of 'mughayara' (difference) and shows that the condition is preserved. This nuance is essential for understanding the logical relationship between premises and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key terms
Identify the terms 'qawlan akhara' and 'mughayara' in the Arabic. The definition hinges on these. Understand that 'qawlan akhara' means a statement other than the premises.
The objection and response revolve around the meaning of 'mughayara'.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that since the conclusion is composed of parts (terms) from the premises, it cannot be 'different'. Recognize that this objection confuses the identity of the proposition with the identity of its components.
The objection is answered by distinguishing between the proposition as a whole and its parts.
3
Examine the response
The author states that 'mughayara' means the conclusion is not identical to either premise as a whole proposition. It does not require that the parts be different from the parts of the premises. Focus on the phrase: 'laysat 'ayna wahida minhuma'.
The example illustrates this: 'Every human is a body' is not the same as 'Every human is an animal' or 'Every animal is a body'.
4
Apply with the example
Take the given example: 'Every human is an animal, Every animal is a body, therefore Every human is a body'. Verify that the conclusion is indeed not identical to either premise. Note that its subject and predicate appear in the premises, but the proposition itself is new.
This demonstrates that the condition of 'qawlan akhara' is satisfied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : قَوْلاً آخَرَا أَيْ: مُغَايراً لِكُلِّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ . 
وَاعْتُرِضَ : بِأَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُتَرَكَّبَةٌ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا تَكُونُ مُغَايرةً .
وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـامُغَايَرَةِ النَّتِيجَةِ لَهُمَا : كَوْنُها لَيْسَتْ عَيْنَ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا ، لَا كَوْنَ أَجْزَائِهَا غَيْرَ أَجْزَائِهِمَا ، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانِ جِسْمٌ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانٍ جِسْمٌ» ، وَهَذِهِ النَّتِيجَةُ مُغَايرَةٌ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِالمَعْنَى المَذْكُورِ، فَافْهَمْ.
His statement: 'qawlan akhara' means different from each of the two premises. And it was objected: that the conclusion must be composed of the parts of the two premises, and then it would not be different. And it was answered: that what is meant by the conclusion's difference from them is that it is not identical to either one of them, not that its parts are different from their parts. So if you say, for example: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body', it yields: 'Every human is a body', and this conclusion is different from the two premises in the mentioned sense. So understand.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage parses the phrase 'qawlan akhara' (another statement) in the definition. It is explained that 'another' means different (mughayir) from each of the two premises. An objection is raised: the conclusion is composed of parts (terms) taken from the premises, so it cannot be truly different. The answer clarifies that 'different' here means not identical to either premise as a whole, not that its parts are different. For example, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is a body', the conclusion 'Every human is a body' is not the same as either premise; its subject and predicate are from the premises, but the proposition as a whole is new. Thus the definition's condition that the syllogism entails 'another statement' is preserved. The commentator ends with 'fa-fham' (so understand), emphasizing the nuance.

Key Terms
مُغَايَرَة
نَتِيجَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The conclusion is not identical to either premise as a whole, though its parts may be from them.
C) The conclusion has a different truth value from the premises.
D) The conclusion must contradict the premises.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a syllogism is 'another statement' because it is a new proposition not identical to any premise, even if it borrows terms from them.

Definition of Qiyas and Its Defense
Bajuri defines qiyas and addresses objections regarding entailment and conclusion distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of qiyas as a composite of premises entailing a conclusion by itself, and defend it against two objections regarding temporal order and propositional identity.

Core Idea
The syllogism is a self-contained machine: given premises, the conclusion necessarily follows and is a new proposition.

In this passage, Bajuri unpacks the classic definition of qiyas from al-Sullam's al-Mantiq. He carefully dissects each phrase: 'composed of premises', 'entailing by itself', 'another statement'. Then he addresses two objections that might confuse a student. By the end, you will see how the definition holds up under scrutiny and how it distinguishes a valid syllogism from a mere list of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its genus
Find the term being defined (qiyas) and the broader category it belongs to: composite of propositions (mu'rafun min qadaya).
This is the first step in any hadd: place the thing in its genus.
2
Extract the differentia: entailment and conclusion
The unique feature is that this composite entails (mustalzim) by itself (bi-dhat) another statement (qawl akhar). Note the three crucial qualifiers: entailment, by itself, another.
The phrase 'by itself' rules out external factors.
3
Analyze the grammatical state (hal) of 'mustalziman'
Bajuri explains that 'mustalziman' is a hal describing the condition of the premises when formed. This addresses the objection that entailment is not simultaneous with formation.
The state indicates concomitant relationship, not necessarily temporal simultaneity.
4
Examine the second objection and its response
The objector says the conclusion shares terms with premises, so it's not truly different. The response: 'different' means as a whole proposition, not that its parts are new.
The example shows the conclusion 'Every human is a body' is a new proposition, though its terms (human, body) appear in the premises.
5
Apply the definition to a sample syllogism
Take the example given: Every human is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore every human is a body. Verify that it meets all parts of the definition.
This is a perfect first-figure syllogism, showing the definition includes both perfect and imperfect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ «القِيَاسَ» مِنْ قَضَايَا صُورًا مُسْتَلْزِمَاً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَا

قَدْ عَرَّفُوا «المُقَدِّمَةَ» بـ: «أَنَّهَا مَا جُعِلَتْ جُزْءَ قِيَاسِ، فَأَخَذُوا القِيَاسَ فِي تَعْرِيفِهَا، فَلَوْ أُخِذَتْ أَيْضاً فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ لَزِمَ الدَّورُ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ .

قَوْلُهُ : (مُسْتَلْزِماً) حَالٌ مِنَ الضَّمِيرِ فِي قَوْلِهِ : صُورًا )) .

وَاعْتُرِضَ : بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ اسْتِلْزَامَ القِيَاسِ لِلنَّتِيجَةِ مَعَ التَّصْوِيرِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُهَا عَقِبَهُ .

وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ عَلَى تَسْلِيمٍ ذَلِكَ نَقُولُ : الحَالِيَّةُ تَدُلُّ عَلَى المُقَارَنَةِ، وَهِيَ فِي كُلِّ شَيْءٍ بِحَسَبِهِ، كَمَا هُوَ شَائِعٌ . وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ : مَا يَعُمُّ البَيِّنَ وَغَيْرَهُ، فَشَمَلَ كَلَامُهُ : المُرَكَّبَ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ: «القِيَاسِ الكَامِلِ». وَالمُرَكَّبَ مِنْ سَائِرِ الأَشْكَالِ، وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ: «غَيْرِ الكَامِلِ». قَوْلُهُ : (بِالذَّاتِ) أَيْ : بِذَاتِهِ ، فَـ «أَلْ» عِوَضٌ عَنِ الضَّمِيرِ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ المُجِيزِ لِذَلِكَ. قَوْلُهُ : قَوْلاً آخَرَا أَيْ: مُغَايراً لِكُلِّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ .

وَاعْتُرِضَ : بِأَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُتَرَكَّبَةٌ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا تَكُونُ مُغَايرةً

وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـامُغَايَرَةِ النَّتِيجَةِ لَهُمَا : كَوْنُها لَيْسَتْ عَيْنَ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهُمَا ، لَا كَوْنَ أَجْزَائِهَا غَيْرَ أَجْزَائِهِمَا ، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانِ جِسْمٌ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانٍ جِسْمٌ» ، وَهَذِهِ النَّتِيجَةُ مُغَايرَةٌ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِالمَعْنَى المَذْكُورِ، فَافْهَمْ.

الأنبابي
قوله : (البَيِّنَ) أي: ما لم يفتقر إلى واسطة، وغيره ما افتقر إليها كتغيير كلِّ مِنَ المقدمتين، أو إحداهما ليرجع القياس إلى الشكل الأول.
Indeed, the syllogism is composed of propositions, when formed, entailing by itself another statement. They have defined the premise as 'that which is made a part of a syllogism', so they included syllogism in its definition; if it were also included in its definition, circularity would result, so reflect. His statement: 'entailing' is a state (hal) from the pronoun in his statement 'when formed'. And it was objected: that this requires that the entailment of the syllogism for the conclusion be simultaneous with its formation, but that is not so, because it only entails it afterwards. And it was answered: even if we grant that, we say: the state (haliyya) indicates concomitance, and it is in everything according to its nature, as is common. It is not hidden that what is meant by 'entailment' is what includes the evident and other than it, so his statement includes: the compound from the first figure, which is called the 'perfect syllogism', and the compound from the remaining figures, which is called the 'imperfect syllogism'. His statement: 'by itself' (bi-dhati), meaning by its essence, so 'al' is a substitute for the pronoun according to those who permit that. His statement: 'another statement' meaning different from each of the two premises. And it was objected: that the conclusion must be composed of parts of the two premises, and then it is not different. And it was answered: what is meant by the conclusion being different from them is that it is not the very same as one of them, not that its parts are different from their parts. So if you say, for example: 'Every human is an animal; every animal is a body', it yields: 'Every human is a body', and this conclusion is different from the two premises in the mentioned sense, so understand. Al-Annababi: His statement: 'the evident' means that which does not need a mediating step, and other than it is that which needs it, such as changing each of the two premises or one of them so that the syllogism reverts to the first figure.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the syllogism (qiyas) as it appears in the matn of al-Sullam. The author, Bajuri, begins by quoting the matn: the syllogism is composed of propositions that, when formed into a specific structure, by their very composition entail a conclusion that is distinct from each of the premises. He then notes a potential circularity issue: logicians define a premise using the term 'syllogism', so if syllogism were defined using 'premise', the circle would be vicious. He leaves the reader to ponder. Next, he analyzes each part of the definition. The phrase 'mustalziman' (entailing) is a grammatical state (hal) describing the condition of the formed propositions. An objection arises: if the entailment is concurrent with the formation, that is false because the conclusion comes after the syllogism is formed. Bajuri answers by clarifying that the state (haliyya) indicates concomitant relationship applicable to each thing according to its nature, and that 'entailment' here includes both immediate and mediated entailments. This covers both perfect syllogisms (first figure, where the conclusion is evident) and imperfect ones (other figures requiring conversion). Then he explains 'bil-dhat' (by itself) as meaning by its own essence, the definite article substituting for the pronoun. Finally, 'qawlan akhara' (another statement) means the conclusion is not identical to either premise. A second objection points out that the conclusion shares its terms with the premises, so it is not entirely different. Bajuri responds that 'different' here means the conclusion as a whole is not one of the premises, not that its components are novel. He illustrates with a classic syllogism: every human is an animal, every animal is a body, therefore every human is a body—the conclusion is a different proposition from the two premises. Al-Annababi adds a note on the term 'al-bayyin' (evident) used earlier: it refers to what requires no mediation (like the first figure), while others require some transformation to be reduced to the first figure.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): A composite of propositions that, when arranged, necessarily entails a conclusion by virtue of its structure. [Note: No significant shift in this stable definition across the tradition.]
مستلزم (mustalzim): Entailing, used as a grammatical state describing the condition of the formed propositions; the entailment is concomitant with the structure.
بالذات (bil-dhat): By itself, meaning the syllogism entails the conclusion purely through its own composition, not via external factors.
قول آخر (qawl akhar): Another statement; the conclusion is not identical to any single premise, though its terms may be drawn from them.
البيّن (al-bayyin): The evident; here referring to the perfect syllogism (first figure) whose conclusion follows without need for conversion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, what does 'another statement' (قول آخر) mean?
A) The conclusion must be entirely composed of new terms not found in the premises.
B) The conclusion is not exactly the same as either of the two premises, even if it shares terms.
C) The conclusion must be a conditional statement.
D) The conclusion is entailed by external factors.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is not just any list of propositions; its specific structure guarantees a new conclusion that follows necessarily from the premises alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism in the first figure on an Islamic topic (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.') and check that it satisfies the definition: composed of premises, entails a conclusion by itself, and the conclusion is a new statement.
?
Identify which part of the definition would be violated if you said: 'Every A is B; every C is D; therefore every A is D.' (Answer: the middle term is missing; the premises are not linked.)

The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
Division of Qiyas into Conjunctive and Exceptive
Bajuri classifies qiyas and defines the conjunctive type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i) syllogisms and explain why the iqtirani is restricted to categorical propositions and indicates the conclusion potentially.

Core Idea
The conjunctive syllogism indicates the conclusion potentially through connected categorical terms.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational division of syllogisms (qiyas) into two types: the conjunctive (iqtirani) and the exceptive (istithna'i). The focus is on understanding the iqtirani, its definition, and its properties as presented by Bajuri. This distinction is crucial for correctly applying logical reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially in categorical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification
The passage begins by stating that qiyas is divided into two types: iqtirani and istithna'i. Note that 'thumma' (then) indicates sequential order in discourse, not time.
2
Extract the definition of iqtirani
The iqtirani is defined as the syllogism that indicates the conclusion by potential strength (bi-quwwa) and is restricted to categorical (hamliyya) propositions. This is the positive definition that gives an existential concept.
3
Understand why it is called 'conjunctive'
It is called iqtirani because its three terms (minor, major, middle) are conjoined without being separated by an exceptive particle (like 'illa' or 'lakenna').
4
Grasp the notion of 'indication by strength'
The conclusion is not explicitly stated as a whole; rather, it is derivable from the premises. This contrasts with the exceptive syllogism where the conclusion or its opposite is explicitly mentioned.
5
Compare with the exceptive syllogism
The exceptive syllogism uses an exceptive particle and sometimes states the conclusion actually (e.g., affirming the antecedent) or its opposite (e.g., denying the consequent). The iqtirani never does so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَقَوْلُهُ : ( عِنْدَهُمْ) أَي : المَنَاطِقَةِ .

قَوْلُهُ : (فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِـ: (الِاقْتِرَانِي يَعْنِي : أَنَّ مِنَ القِيَاسِ قِسْماً يُسَمَّى بـ: (الِاقْتِرَانِي»؛ لاِقْتِرَانِ حُدُودِهِ، وَاتِّصَالِ بَعْضِهَا بِبَعْضِ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَصْلِ بَيْنَهُمَا بِأَدَاةِ الِاسْتِثْنَاءِ؛ الَّتِي هِيَ وَسَيَأْتِي قَسِيمُ ذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِالِاسْتِثْنَائِي . .. إلخ». قَوْلُهُ : (وَهُوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ ... إلخ) عَلَى هَذَا التَّعْرِيفِ يَكُونُ مَفْهُومُهُ وُجُودِيًّا، بِخِلَافِهِ عَلَى تَعْرِيفِهِ «أَنَّهُ الَّذِي لَمْ تُذْكَرْ فِيهِ النَّتيجَةُ وَلَا نَقِيضُهَا بِالفِعْلِ

وَالأَوَّلُ أَوْلَى ؛ لإيهام الثَّانِي أَنَّهُ قَدْ يُذْكَرُ فِيهِ نَقِيضُ النَّتِيجَةِ بِالقُوَّةِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ؛ لَكِنَّ الحَامِلَ لَهُمْ عَلَى ذَلِكَ المُقَابَلَةُ بِالاسْتِثْنَائِي، وَهُوَ تَارَةً تُذْكَرُ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ بِالفِعْلِ (۱)، وَتَارَةً يُذْكَرُ فِيهِ نَقِيضُهَا كَذَلِكَ (٢) ، كَمَا سَيَأْتِي بَيَانُهُ .

قوله : (حُدُودِهِ) أي : الثَّلاثة الأصغر والأكبر والأوسط، وسميت : «حدوداً»؛ لأنَّها أطراف، والحد

لغة: «الطرف».

(1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (وَهُوَ نَارَةٌ تُذْكَرُ ... إلخ) وذلك إذا استثنيت عين المقدَّم ؛ بأن قلت في قولك: «لَوْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، كَانَ حَيَوَاناً » : «لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ، فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ» .

(۲) الشرشيمي: قوله : (نَقِيضُهَا) وذلك إن استثنيت نقيض التَّالي ؛ بأن قلت : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ»؛ أي: «فَلَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ»، فقولك : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ» نقيض : «هُوَ حَيَوَانٌ ، وإن كان ذلك النقيض لم يذكر على وجه النتيجة، بل هو المقدمة الصُّغرى في القياس؛ وفيه بعد، أمَّا أوّلاً فلأنَّ الكلام في الاقتراني، وأما ثانياً : فإنَّ قوله : «وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغرى ... إلخ» خاص بالاقتراني؛ بناءً على ظاهر الاستثناء من عدم تأويله بالاقتراني، وإن كان قوله : «فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَه ... إلخ يتحقق فيهما .
Then the syllogism, according to them, is two divisions: among it is that which is called the conjunctive (iqtirani), and it is that which indicates the conclusion by strength and is restricted to categorical (hamliyya). His statement: 'Then the syllogism... etc.' 'Then' is for sequential mention. His statement: 'according to them' means the logicians. His statement: 'among it is that which is called the conjunctive' means that there is a type of syllogism called 'conjunctive' due to the conjunction of its limits (terms) and the connection of some of them with others without separation by an exceptive particle, which is the one whose counterpart will come in his statement: 'and among it is that which is called the exceptive... etc.' His statement: 'and it is that which indicates... etc.' According to this definition, its concept is existential, unlike its definition as 'that in which the conclusion or its opposite is not mentioned actually'. The first is preferable because the second gives the impression that the opposite of the conclusion might be mentioned potentially, which is not the case; however, what compelled them to that is the comparison with the exceptive syllogism, in which sometimes the conclusion is mentioned actually (1), and sometimes its opposite is mentioned as such (2), as will be explained. And indeed the indication of the conjunctive syllogism of the conclusion is by potential strength (bi-quwwa), not actual (bi'l-fi'l), because the conclusion is not mentioned in it in its explicit form, although it is mentioned in it dispersed. His statement: 'and is restricted to categorical' means that it is confined to categorical propositions and does not extend beyond them, so the 'ba'... Al-Annababi: His statement: 'its limits (hudud)' means the three: the minor, the major, and the middle; and they are called 'limits' because they are ends (atraf), and 'hadd' linguistically means 'end' (taraf). (1) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: 'and it is sometimes mentioned... etc.' That is when you except the exact antecedent, as when you say in your statement: 'If this were human, it would be an animal': 'but it is human, so it is an animal.' (2) Al-Shirshimi: His statement: 'its opposite' that is by excepting the opposite of the consequent, as when you say: 'but it is not an animal'; i.e., 'so it is not a human'. So your statement 'but it is not an animal' is the opposite of 'it is an animal', although that opposite is not mentioned as a conclusion, rather it is the minor premise in the syllogism; yet there is some distance in that. First, because the discussion is about the conjunctive syllogism. Second, because his statement: 'and none of the premises is minor... etc.' is specific to the conjunctive, based on the apparent sense of the exception without interpreting it as conjunctive, although his statement: 'if you want to combine it... etc.' would be realized in both.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of syllogism (qiyas) into two types: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The matn line states that the syllogism is divided, and one type is called iqtirani, which indicates the conclusion by virtue of its inherent strength and is restricted to categorical (subject-predicate) propositions. Bajuri then explicates the text. The word 'thumma' (then) indicates sequential order in discourse, not temporal. 'According to them' refers to the logicians. The name 'iqtirani' comes from the conjunction (iqtiran) of its terms (hudud) — that is, the minor, major, and middle terms are connected without being separated by an exceptive particle like 'illa' or 'lakenna'. This distinguishes it from the exceptive syllogism. Bajuri then notes two definitions of iqtirani: one is the given definition (indicates by strength, restricted to categoricals), which gives a positive (wujudiyya) concept; the other is a negative definition (that it does not explicitly state the conclusion or its opposite). The positive definition is superior because the negative one might wrongly suggest that the opposite of the conclusion could be potentially present. The reason logicians sometimes use the negative definition is for contrast with the exceptive syllogism, which sometimes explicitly states the conclusion (e.g., if you affirm the antecedent in a conditional) or its opposite (e.g., if you deny the consequent). The conclusion of iqtirani is indicated potentially (bi-quwwa), meaning it is not stated explicitly as a single proposition but can be derived from the premises, even if its terms appear scattered across them. The restriction to categorical propositions means iqtirani deals only with hamliyya (categorical) propositions, not conditional or disjunctive ones. Annababi adds that 'hudud' (limits) refer to the three terms: minor (asghar), major (akbar), and middle (awsat); they are called 'limits' linguistically meaning 'ends'. Finally, al-Shirshimi provides footnotes giving examples of exceptive syllogisms: affirming the antecedent ('If this is human, it is an animal; but it is human, so it is an animal') and denying the consequent ('If this is human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal, so it is not human'). He notes that these examples relate to the exceptive syllogism, not the conjunctive, and there is some subtlety about whether the statement 'none of the premises is minor' applies to the conjunctive only, but clarifies that the discussion is about iqtirani.

Why is the iqtirani syllogism called 'conjunctive'?
A) Because it combines multiple conclusions into one.

Key Takeaway
The conjunctive syllogism is characterized by connected categorical terms indicating the conclusion potentially, setting it apart from the exceptive syllogism that uses an exceptive particle and may state the conclusion explicitly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the following argument: 'If the sun rises, then it is day; the sun has risen, so it is day.' Identify the type of syllogism and explain why it is not iqtirani.
?

The Syllogism
beginner
8 min
Definition of Conjunctive Syllogism and Its Composition
Defines conjunctive syllogism and expands on its possible compositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the restricted and extended definitions of the conjunctive syllogism and identify its five possible compositions.

Core Idea
The conjunctive syllogism is not limited to categorical propositions; it can be composed from conditionals in five ways.

In this passage, the author first defines the conjunctive syllogism (qiyās iqtirānī) as a syllogism that indicates its conclusion potentially and is initially said to be specific to categorical propositions. However, he then presents the majority view that it extends to conditional propositions as well. By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate and be able to enumerate the five compositions of iqtirani and recognize an example in the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of iqtirani
Locate the phrase where iqtirani is defined: it indicates the conclusion potentially (bi-quwwa) and is specific to categorical (hamliyya) propositions. 'Bi-quwwa' means the conclusion is implicit, not stated explicitly.
The definition here is the author's own claim; note that it will be challenged by the majority view.
2
Recognize the initial restriction
The author explicitly states that iqtirani is confined to categorical propositions (مقصور على الحملية). Understand what categorical propositions are: simple subject-predicate statements like 'Every A is B'.
3
Understand the majority view
The majority of logicians disagree with the restriction. They hold that iqtirani can be composed from conditional propositions (shartiyya) as well, including both connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) types.
This expands the scope of iqtirani significantly.
4
Enumerate the five compositions
List the five divisions as given: (1) two connected conditionals, (2) two disjunctives, (3) a connected and a disjunctive, (4) a categorical and a connected, (5) a categorical and a disjunctive. Commit these to memory.
These five cover all possible pairings of the two types of propositions.
5
Analyze the example
The passage gives an example of the first division in the first figure: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; and it is not the case that if daytime exists, night occurs; therefore, it is not the case that if the sun is rising, night occurs.' Verify that this matches the form: If P then Q; Not (if Q then R); therefore, Not (if P then R).
This shows that conditional iqtirani syllogisms are valid.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْقِيَاسُ عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِالِاقْتِرَانِي وَهُوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ بِقُوَّةٍ وَاخْتَصَّ بِالْحَمْلِيَّةِ. وَاخْتَصَّ بِالْحَمْلِيَّةِ يَعْنِي أَنَّهُ مَقْصُورٌ عَلَى الْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَلَا يَتَعَدَّاهَا إِلَى غَيْرِهَا. وَالَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الْجُمْهُورُ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْتَصُّ بِهَا بَلْ قَدْ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ وَيَنْتَظِمُ فِيهِ حِينَئِذٍ خَمْسَةُ أَقْسَامٍ: الْأَوَّلُ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ شَرْطِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ، وَالثَّانِي الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ شَرْطِيَتَيْنِ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ، وَالثَّالِثُ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةٍ وَشَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ، وَالرَّابِعُ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَشَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَالْخَامِسُ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَشَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ. وَيَنْعَقِدُ فِي كُلِّ قِسْمٍ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْأَقْسَامِ الْخَمْسَةِ الْأَشْكَالُ الْأَرْبَعَةُ؛ مِثَالُ الْقِسْمِ الْأَوَّلِ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةً فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ، وَلَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ النَّهَارُ مَوْجُودًا فَاللَّيْلُ حَاصِلٌ، يَنْتُجُ: لَيْسَ الْبَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةً فَاللَّيْلُ حَاصِلٌ.
Then the syllogism according to them is two divisions: among them is what is called the conjunctive (iqtirani), which is that which indicates the conclusion potentially and is specific to categorical propositions. And it is specific to categorical means that it is confined to categorical and does not extend to other than it. And the view of the majority is that it is not specific to it, but rather it may be composed from conditional propositions, and in that case five divisions are organized in it: first, composed of two connected conditionals; second, composed of two disjunctive conditionals; third, composed of a connected conditional and a disjunctive conditional; fourth, composed of a categorical and a connected conditional; fifth, composed of a categorical and a disjunctive conditional. And in each of these five divisions the four figures are valid; an example of the first division of the first figure is: Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; and it is absolutely not the case that if daytime exists, night occurs; it yields: It is absolutely not the case that if the sun is rising, night occurs.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines the conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani) and then expands on the debate about its scope. The author first states that qiyas is divided into two types: iqtirani and istithna'i. The iqtirani is defined as that which indicates the conclusion potentially (bi-quwwa), not actually, because the conclusion is not mentioned explicitly; it is implicit in the premises. Then the author adds that it is specific to categorical (hamli) propositions, i.e., those with subject-predicate structure. The commentator notes that this specification is the author's view, shared by Ibn al-Hajib. However, the majority of logicians hold that iqtirani is not limited to categoricals; it can also be composed from conditional (shartiyya) propositions—both connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The majority enumerate five possible compositions: (1) two connected conditionals, (2) two disjunctives, (3) a connected and a disjunctive, (4) a categorical and a connected, and (5) a categorical and a disjunctive. The commentator then explains that within each of these five compositions, all four figures of the syllogism can be formed. An example is given for the first division in the first figure: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' (major premise), and 'It is not the case that if daytime exists, night occurs' (minor premise), yielding 'It is not the case that if the sun is rising, night occurs.' This shows the possibility of conditional conjunctive syllogisms. The passage thus clarifies that the definition of iqtirani is broader than just categorical propositions according to the dominant view.

Definition of Qiyas Iqtirani and Its Five Compound Types
Definition of qiyas iqtirani and five compound types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qiyas iqtirani, identify the positions of the author and the majority, and enumerate the five compound types from conditional premises.

Core Idea
Qiyas iqtirani is not confined to categoricals; the majority allows five compound types with conditionals.

The passage defines qiyas iqtirani as a conjunctive syllogism that indicates the conclusion with strength. A key debate is whether it is restricted to categorical premises or can include conditionals. The author (like Ibn al-Hajib) holds the restrictive view, but the majority logicians accept five compound types when conditionals are included. Understanding this debate clarifies the flexibility of syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debated point
Read the definition carefully. The author says qiyas iqtirani is 'confined to the categorical'. What does this mean? Note that the majority of logicians disagree.
This is a point of contention in the logical tradition.
2
Understand the two positions
The author and Ibn al-Hajib restrict qiyas iqtirani to categorical premises. The majority hold that it can be composed from conditional premises, yielding five types. Why might the author disregard compound conditionals? The passage suggests 'lack of utility'.
Consider the practical implications for argumentation.
3
Memorize the five compound types
The five types are: (1) two connected conditionals, (2) two disconnected conditionals, (3) one connected and one disconnected, (4) a categorical and a connected conditional, (5) a categorical and a disconnected conditional. Write them down with a mnemonic.
These cover all combinations of categorical and conditional premises.
4
Work through the example
The passage gives an example of the first type in the first figure: 'Whenever the sun is shining, day exists; absolutely not: when day exists, night occurs' yields 'absolutely not: whenever the sun is shining, night occurs'. Identify the premises and conclusion. This is a conditional syllogism with negation.
The example uses connected conditionals and produces a negative conclusion.

Chapter on Syllogism. (75) And it is that which indicates the conclusion with strength and is confined to the categorical. Al-Bajuri: (the word 'confined') enters upon that which is restricted to it, even if it is against the usual as previously. And this is what the author held, like Ibn al-Hajib. And the majority view is that it is not confined to it, rather it may be composed from conditional (premises), and then there are five divisions: (1) First: composed of two connected conditionals. (2) Second: composed of two disconnected conditionals. (3) Third: composed of a connected conditional and a disconnected conditional. Commentary
This passage begins by defining qiyas iqtirani (conjunctive syllogism) as that which leads to a conclusion with strength, and that according to the author (and Ibn al-Hajib) it is confined to categorical (hamli) premises. However, the majority of logicians hold that it can also be composed from conditional premises, yielding five types. The author enumerates them: two conditionals both connected (mutasila), both disconnected (munfasila), one connected and one disconnected, a categorical with a connected conditional, and a categorical with a disconnected conditional. He then notes that each of these five types can be arranged into the four figures (shakl). He gives an example of the first type in the first figure: a conditional syllogism with two connected conditionals, where the middle term is the consequent in the first and antecedent in the second, leading to a conclusion that is the negation of the original antecedent. He refers detailed examples to longer works. Finally, he suggests that perhaps the author (and Ibn al-Hajib) disregarded the compound conditional syllogism due to its limited benefit.

Examples of Compound Conditional Syllogisms: Types 2, 3, and 5
Commentator provides examples for three of the five compound types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a compound conditional syllogism into one of the five types based on the nature of its premises.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals combine multiple premise types to yield conclusions not possible with simple syllogisms.

In the previous lesson, you learned the five types of compound conditional syllogisms. Now, the commentator provides concrete examples for three of them: the second (two disconnected conditionals), the third (a connected conditional and a categorical), and the fifth (a disconnected conditional and a categorical). By studying these examples, you will recognize how the types differ in structure and how the conclusion follows from the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
For each example, determine whether each premise is a connected conditional (if...then), a disconnected conditional (either...or), or a categorical (every...is).
The passage labels premises as 'disconnected' or 'connected' in context.
2
Match to the compound type
Based on the combination of premise types, identify which of the five compound types the syllogism belongs to.
e.g., two disconnected = type 2; connected + categorical = type 3; disconnected + categorical = type 5.
3
Extract the conclusion
See how the conclusion is formed by combining or eliminating terms from the premises.
For type 2, the conclusion is a three-way disjunction; for type 3, it is a connected conditional; for type 5, it is a disjunction.
4
Test the validity
Check if the conclusion necessarily follows from the premises given the logical rules of each type.
These examples are all valid as per the rules of compound conditionals.

الأنبابي
قوله : ( وَالثَّانِي ... إلخ) مثاله : كُلُّ عَدَدٍ : إِمَّا زَوْجٌ أَوْ فَرْدُ، وَكُلُّ زَوْجِ: إِمَّا زَوْجُ الزَّوْجِ، أَوْ زَوْجُ الفَرْدِ»، ونتيجتُهُ: كُلُّ عَدَدٍ : إِمَّا فَرْدٌ ، أَوْ زَوْجُ الزَّوْج ، أَوْ زَوْجُ الفَرْدِ»، وزوجُ الزَّوجُ هو المنقسم إلى زوجين، وزوج الفرد: ما لم ينقسم إليهما . قوله : ( وَالثَّالِثُ ... إلخ) مثالُهُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ ينتج : كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً ، فَهُوَ جِسْم.

Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'And the second... etc.' Example: 'Every number is either even or odd, and every even is either even of even or even of odd'; and its conclusion: 'Every number is either odd or even of even or even of odd.' And even of even is that which is divisible into two evens, and even of odd is that which is not divisible into them. His statement: 'And the third... etc.' Example: 'Whenever this thing is a human then it is an animal, and every animal is a body' yields: 'Whenever this thing is a human then it is a body.' His statement: 'And the fifth... etc.' Example: 'Every number is either even or odd, and every even is divisible into equals' yields: 'Every number is either odd or divisible into equals.'

Commentary

The examples are from mathematics and ontology. For the second type, the premises are two disjunctive conditionals, and the conclusion is a disjunction of three terms. For the third, a connected conditional (if human then animal) and a categorical universal (every animal is body) produce a connected conditional. For the fifth, a disjunctive and a categorical produce a disjunction. The commentator also defines technical terms like 'even of even' and 'even of odd'.

The Syllogism

Four Figures in Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains the four figures for compound conditional syllogisms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which figure a compound conditional syllogism belongs to based on the position of the middle term as antecedent or consequent.

Core Idea
The four figures of syllogisms apply to compound conditionals by tracking the middle term's role.

The author extends the familiar four figures from categorical syllogisms to compound conditional syllogisms. By understanding how the middle term (hadd wasat) is positioned as antecedent (muqaddam) or consequent (talin) in each premise, you can determine the figure. This lesson walks through each figure with concrete examples involving connected conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In a compound conditional syllogism, the middle term is the proposition that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It can be either an antecedent or a consequent in each premise.
The middle term is not the whole conditional proposition but a part of it.
2
Determine its position in the minor premise
Look at the minor (first) premise: is the middle term the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (talin)?
Example: In 'Whenever the sun is shining, day exists', 'sun is shining' is antecedent, 'day exists' is consequent.
3
Determine its position in the major premise
Look at the major (second) premise: is the middle term the antecedent or the consequent?
The major premise may start with 'whenever' or 'if'.
4
Match the pair to the figure
Now combine the two positions: (minor, major). First figure: consequent in minor, antecedent in major. Second: consequent in both. Third: antecedent in both. Fourth: antecedent in minor, consequent in major.
Memorize the four patterns to quickly classify any compound conditional syllogism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَيَنْعَقِدُ . .. إلخ) بيان ذلك : - أنَّ الحدّ الوسط إن كان تالياً في الصُّغرى مقدَّماً في الكبرى، فهو الشكل الأوَّل؛ كما في مثال المحشّي. وإن كان تالياً فيهما ، فهو الشكل الثاني؛ كقولك في الشَّرطي المركَّب من متصلتين الاقتراني: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ، وَلَيْسَ البنّةَ إِذَا كَانَ اللَّيْلُ حَاصِلاً فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ ) . وإن كان مقدماً فيهما ، فهو الشكل الثالث؛ كقولك منه في القياس المذكور: «كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالأَرْضُ مُضِينَةٌ . وإن كان مقدَّماً في الصُّغرى تالياً في الكبرى، فالرَّابعُ؛ كقولك منه في القياس المذكور: «كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةً فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ ، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الأَرْضُ مُضِينَةٌ فَالشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ . وبيان نتائج هذه الأشكال مع بيان الأمثلة لا يليق هنا
His statement: 'And there occur... etc.' Explanation: That the middle term if it is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major, then it is the first figure; as in the example of the marginal annotator. And if it is consequent in both, then it is the second figure; like your saying in the compound conditional syllogism from two connected conditionals: 'Whenever the sun is shining then day exists, and absolutely (not) when night occurs then day exists.' And if it is antecedent in both, then it is the third figure; like your saying in the mentioned syllogism: 'Whenever the sun is shining then day exists, and whenever the sun is shining then the earth is illuminated.' And if it is antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major, then the fourth; like your saying in the mentioned syllogism: 'Whenever the sun is shining then day exists, and whenever the earth is illuminated then the sun is shining.' And elaboration of the conclusions of these figures with the examples is not suitable here.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage explains how the four figures of syllogisms apply to compound conditional syllogisms. The middle term (hadd wasat) can occupy four positions: (1) First figure: middle is consequent in the minor premise and antecedent in the major. (2) Second figure: middle is consequent in both premises. (3) Third figure: middle is antecedent in both premises. (4) Fourth figure: middle is antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major. Each is illustrated with an example involving connected conditionals from the first type (two connected conditionals). The commentator notes that elaborating the conclusions and further examples is beyond the scope of this work.

The four figures of compound conditional syllogisms are defined solely by the position of the middle term as antecedent or consequent in the two premises.

The Syllogism
beginner

Ordering Premises in Syllogisms
Rules for arranging syllogism premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct order of premises in a conjunctive (categorical) versus an exceptional (conditional) syllogism.

Core Idea
In conjunctive syllogisms, minor precedes major; in exceptional, the conditional major comes first.

Knowing the correct order of premises is essential for constructing a valid syllogism. The classical logicians established a clear rule: in a conjunctive (categorical) syllogism, the minor premise precedes the major, whereas in an exceptional (conditional) syllogism, the order is reversed. This lesson unpacks that rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of syllogisms
Identify whether the syllogism is conjunctive (categorical, with simple propositions) or exceptional (conditional, with a conditional major and an exceptional minor).
2
Recall the rule for each type
For conjunctive syllogisms: minor premise first, then major. For exceptional syllogisms: major (the conditional) first, then the exceptional minor.
The source states 'present the minor before the major in the conjunctive syllogism... and the opposite in the exceptional.'
3
Analyze the example given
Take the example: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body.' In this conjunctive syllogism, the first premise (human-animal) is the minor because it contains the subject of the conclusion, and the second (animal-body) is the major. This matches the rule.
The conclusion would be 'Every human is a body.'
4
Understand the reason for reversal
In exceptional syllogisms, the major premise is a conditional (e.g., 'If A then B'), and the minor is the exception (e.g., 'But not B' or 'A occurs'). Placing the conditional first clarifies the logical dependency before the exception is applied.
The order ensures clarity and follows established convention.
5
Check premises for soundness
Besides order, premises must be 'sound' – meeting conditions for productivity (e.g., truth, proper terms). The verse commands to examine sound from corrupt, meaning verify each premise before using it.
Al-Shirshimi notes that soundness includes material truth and formal conditions.

الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ ... إلخ) مُقْتَضَى السَّيَاقِ : أَنَّ الصَّمِيرَ رَاجِعُ إِلَى القِيَاسِ الِاقْتِرَانِيِّ؛ لِأَنَّ كَلَامَ الْمُصَنِّفِ بِصَدَدِهِ، لَكِنَّ الَّذِي قَالَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ رَاجِعٌ إِلَى القِيَاسِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ؛ لِأَنَّ مَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ المُصَنِّفُ غَيْرَ مُخْتَصّ بِالاقْتِرَانِي، وَفِيهِ بُعْدُ لَا يَخْفَى.
قَوْلُهُ : (مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ) المُرَادُ بِالجَمْعِ هُنَا وَفِيمَا بَعْدُ: مَا فَوْقَ الوَاحِدِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا أَيْ: عَلَى الوَجْهِ الَّذِي وَجَبَ عِنْدَهُمْ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ شَامِلٌ لِجَمِيعِ مَا وَجَبَ، فَقَوْلُهُ : (وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ ... إلخ، مِنْ ذِكْرِ الخَاصٌ بَعْدَ العَامّ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ تَخْصِيصُ مَا هُنَا بِغَيرِ مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ بَعْدُ كَالإِتيَانِ بِالحَدِّ الوَسَطِ .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ... إلخ) أَيْ : بِأَنْ تُقَدِّمَ الصُّغْرَى عَلَى الكُبْرَى فِي القِيَاسِ الِاقْتِرَائِي؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ ، وَبِالعَكْسِ فِي الِاسْتِثْنَائِي كَمَا سَيَأْتِي.

الشرشيمي : قوله : (مِنْ ذِكْرِ الخَاصٌ بَعْدَ العَامُ) حتَّى قوله : ( وَانْظُرَا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ) وقوله : (مِنْ فَاسِد» متعلق بمحذوف؛ أي: متميّزاً من فاسد لكن قول المحشّي في تفسير الصحيح : بأن يكون مستجمعاً لشروط الإنتاج» يفيد : أنَّه مسا و لما هنا ، بل هو أعم باعتبار اشتماله على المادَّة أيضاً، والنظر في الصحة قبل التركيب، كما أنَّ الاختيار كذلك
(76) So if you intend to compose it, compose its premises according to what is necessary. (77) And arrange the premises and examine the sound from the corrupt as one who tests. Al-Bajuri: His statement: 'So if you intend to compose it...' etc. The context requires that the pronoun refers to the conjunctive syllogism, because the author's speech is about it, but some say it refers to the syllogism as such, because what the author will mention is not exclusive to the conjunctive, and in that there is an obvious distance. His statement: 'its premises' means by the plural here and hereafter: more than one. His statement: 'according to what is necessary' i.e., in the manner that is necessary according to them, and it is not hidden that that includes all that is necessary, so his statement 'and arrange the premises...' etc. is from mentioning the specific after the general, and it is possible that what is here is specified by other than what is clarified later, like bringing the middle term. His statement: 'and arrange the premises...' etc. i.e., by presenting the minor before the major in the conjunctive syllogism; as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body'; and the opposite in the exceptional (conditional) syllogism as will come. His statement: 'and examine the sound from the corrupt' i.e., examine the sound ones from among them from the corrupt. Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'and the opposite in the exceptional (syllogism)' i.e., because the major in the exceptional is the conditional, and the minor is the exceptional. Al-Shirshimi: His statement: 'from mentioning the specific after the general...' etc.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two verses of the matn that provide instructions for constructing a syllogism. The first verse (76) tells the reader to compose the premises as required. The second (77) commands arranging the premises and distinguishing sound from corrupt. Al-Bajuri begins by discussing the referent of the pronoun in 'compose it' – whether it refers specifically to the conjunctive syllogism or to syllogisms in general. He notes the majority view that it is general, but finds it distant. He then explains that 'premises' (muqaddimat) means more than one. He interprets 'according to what is necessary' as the established rules. He notes that 'arrange the premises' is a specific command after a general one, and may imply ordering the minor before the major in conjunctive syllogisms, giving the classic example 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body'. He adds that in exceptional (conditional) syllogisms, the order is reversed: the conditional major comes first, then the minor exception. Al-Anbabi confirms this with an explanation of the structure of exceptional syllogisms. Finally, al-Shirshimi provides a marginal note clarifying that the phrase 'examine the sound from the corrupt' requires examining premises that meet the conditions of productivity (intaj).

Compounding the Syllogism: Referent and Scope
Author instructs on constructing a compound syllogism and its premises correctly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the referent of the pronoun in a logical text and distinguish between general and specific instructions for syllogism construction.

Core Idea
A pronoun's referent determines whether a rule applies to all syllogisms or only categorical ones.

When a commentator like al-Bajuri explains a verse about 'compounding it,' the first task is to determine what 'it' refers to. This lesson walks you through that interpretive process, showing how a seemingly small point about pronoun reference can affect the scope of a logical rule. You will also see how the commentator distinguishes general requirements from specific applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pronoun referent in the verse
Read the verse: 'If you intend to compound it, then compose its premises as required.' Ask: What does 'it' (in 'compound it') refer to? The immediate context – the preceding discussion about categorical syllogisms – suggests it is the categorical syllogism. But some argue it refers to the syllogism in general. Compare both possibilities.
The pronoun 'hu' in 'tarkībahū' is ambiguous; context and later remarks help resolve it.
2
Evaluate the disagreement among commentators
Al-Bajuri reports that 'some said' the pronoun refers to the syllogism generally, not just categorical. He finds this view weak ('fīhi bu'dun la yakhfā'). Understand why: the author's subsequent discussion is focused on the categorical syllogism, and generalizing the rule would expand its application inappropriately.
This disagreement shows how careful interpretation is needed even at the lexical level.
3
Interpret the plural 'premises' as at least two
The verse says 'its premises' (muqaddimātihi). Al-Bajuri notes that the plural here means 'more than one' – at least two premises, which is typical for a syllogism. Recognize that a compound syllogism may have more than two premises, but the minimum is two.
The Arabic plural often indicates three or more, but here it includes two.
4
Distinguish general obligation from specific application
The phrase 'as required' (ʿalā mā wajabā) is a general statement covering everything obligatory in syllogism construction. The next instruction 'arrange the premises' is one specific application. Al-Bajuri says mentioning the specific after the general is a common rhetorical device. Also, later instructions like 'bringing the middle term' specify what is needed for categorical syllogisms.
This helps you see how a commentator expands a concise verse into detailed rules.

(٧٦) فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ فَرَكْبَا مُقَدِّمَانِهِ عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا. قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ ... إلخ) مُقْتَضَى السَّيَاقِ : أَنَّ الصَّمِيرَ رَاجِعُ إِلَى القِيَاسِ الِاقْتِرَانِيِّ؛ لِأَنَّ كَلَامَ الْمُصَنِّفِ بِصَدَدِهِ، لَكِنَّ الَّذِي قَالَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ رَاجِعٌ إِلَى القِيَاسِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ ؛ لِأَنَّ مَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ المُصَنِّفُ غَيْرَ مُخْتَصّ بِالاقْتِرَانِي، وَفِيهِ بُعْدُ لَا يَخْفَى. قَوْلُهُ : (مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ) المُرَادُ بِالجَمْعِ هُنَا وَفِيمَا بَعْدُ: مَا فَوْقَ الوَاحِدِ. قَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا أَيْ: عَلَى الوَجْهِ الَّذِي وَجَبَ عِنْدَهُمْ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ شَامِلٌ لِجَمِيعِ مَا وَجَبَ، فَقَوْلُهُ : (وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ ... إلخ، مِنْ ذِكْرِ الخَاصٌ بَعْدَ العَامّ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ تَخْصِيصُ مَا هُنَا بِغَيرِ مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ بَعْدُ كَالإِتيَانِ بِالحَدِّ الوَسَطِ .
(76) If you intend to compound it, then compose its two premises as required. His statement: 'If you intend to compound it...' etc. The context requires that the pronoun refers to the categorical syllogism because the author's speech is about it. However, some said that it refers to the syllogism as such, because what the author will mention is not specific to the categorical, but there is an obvious remoteness in this. His statement: 'its premises' — the plural here and afterwards means more than one. His statement: 'as required' i.e. in the manner that is obligatory according to them. It is not hidden that this includes everything obligatory. So his statement: 'And arrange the premises...' etc. is mentioning the specific after the general. And it is possible that what is here is specified by what he explicitly states afterwards, such as bringing the middle term.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse that instructs the student on constructing a compound syllogism. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by clarifying the referent of the pronoun in 'compounding it.' He notes that the context of the author's discussion about the categorical syllogism suggests the pronoun refers back to the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). However, some scholars dissent, holding that it refers to the syllogism in general, since the author will later discuss topics not restricted to the categorical form. Al-Bajuri finds this view weak. He then explains that 'its premises' (muqaddimatihi) indicates at least two premises, as the plural is used. The phrase 'as required' (ʿalā mā wajabā) means in the manner recognized as obligatory by the logicians. The commentator observes that this is a general statement, and the subsequent line about arranging premises is a specific application. He also suggests that the general statement here may be specified by later instructions, such as the requirement to supply the middle term in categorical syllogisms.

The Syllogism

Ordering Premises in Syllogisms
Instructions on ordering premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly order premises in categorical and exceptional syllogisms according to the rule given by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Minor before major in categorical; reversed in exceptional.

In constructing a syllogism, the order of premises is not arbitrary. Al-Bajuri explains the rule for ordering in different types of syllogisms, which is essential for proper logical form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism type
Determine whether the syllogism is categorical (iqtirani) or exceptional (istithna'i). The rule for premise order differs.
The passage explicitly states that the rule for categorical is given here, and the rule for exceptional will come later.
2
Locate the minor and major premises
The minor premise (ṣughrā) is the one whose subject is the subject of the conclusion. The major premise (kubrā) has the predicate of the conclusion.
In the example 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body', the minor is 'Every human is an animal' and the major is 'Every animal is a body'.
3
Apply the ordering rule
For a categorical syllogism, place the minor premise before the major. For an exceptional syllogism, the order is reversed (major first).
The commentator emphasizes this as the way to 'arrange the premises'.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Construct a categorical syllogism from fiqh or kalam and ensure the minor premise comes first.
Example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (major); every nabidh is an intoxicant (minor) → conclusion: nabidh is forbidden.' But note the minor should be first: 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant; every intoxicant is forbidden.

(۷۷) وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَانْظُرَا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ مُخْتَبِرًا. قَوْلُهُ : (وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ... إلخ) أَيْ : بِأَنْ تُقَدِّمَ الصُّغْرَى عَلَى الْكُبْرَى فِي الْقِيَاسِ الِاقْتِرَانِيِّ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ، وَبِالعَكْسِ فِي الِاسْتِثْنَائِي كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. قَوْلُهُ : (وَانْظُرَا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ) أي : انْظُرِ الصَّحِيحَ مِنْهَا مِنَ الْفَاسِدِ . وَالأَوَّلُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ صَحِيحاً مِنْ جِهَةِ الْمَادَّةِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صَادِقاً، وَمِنْ جِهَةِ النَّظْم بِأَنْ يَكُونَ مُسْتَجْمِعاً لِشُرُوطِ الْإِنْتَاجِ، وَبِذَلِكَ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ الْفَاسِدَ شَامِلٌ لِلْفَاسِدِ مِنْ جِهَةِ الْمَادَّةِ بِأَنْ كَانَ كَاذِباً، وَلِلْفَاسِدِ مِنْ جِهَةِ النَّظْمِ بِأَنْ كَانَ غَيْرَ مُسْتَجْمِعٍ لِشُرُوطِ الْإِنْتَاجِ. قَوْلُهُ : (مُخْتَبِرًا) أَيْ: حَالَ كَوْنِكَ مُخْتَبِراً لَهَا هَلْ هِيَ يَقِينِيَّةٌ، أَوْ ظَنِّيَّةٌ؟ وَهَلْ هِيَ عَلَى تَأْلِيفٍ منتج، أَوْ لَا؟
(77) And arrange the premises and look at the sound from the unsound while testing. His statement: 'And arrange the premises...' etc., i.e., by placing the minor before the major in the categorical syllogism, as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body.' And conversely in the exceptional syllogism as will come. His statement: 'and look at the sound from the unsound' i.e., look at the sound among them from the unsound. The first must be sound in terms of matter, i.e., true, and in terms of form, i.e., satisfying the conditions of production. By that it is known that the unsound includes the unsound in terms of matter, i.e., false, and the unsound in terms of form, i.e., not satisfying the conditions of production. His statement: 'while testing' i.e., while you are testing them: are they certain or probable? And are they arranged productively or not?
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse that gives two instructions for constructing a syllogism: arranging the premises and distinguishing sound from unsound ones. The commentator al-Bajuri explains that 'arranging the premises' means ordering the minor premise before the major premise in the categorical syllogism, giving the example 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body.' In the exceptional (conditional) syllogism, the order is reversed, as will be explained later. Then he explains 'looking at the sound from the unsound' — the sound premise must be sound in matter (materially true) and in form (formally valid, satisfying the conditions of production). Conversely, the unsound includes both material falsity and formal invalidity. Finally, 'while testing' means examining whether the premises are certain or probable, and whether their arrangement is productive. Al-Bajuri notes that this testing is part of the process of distinguishing sound from unsound.

Key Terms
الصغرى
الكبرى

Checkpoint
In a categorical syllogism, which premise should be placed first according to al-Bajuri's commentary?
A) The major premise
B) The minor premise
C) The middle term
D) The conclusion

Key Takeaway
The order of premises is a key formal condition: minor before major in categorical syllogisms, and reversed in exceptional ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism from fiqh using the example of qiyas on riba or khamr. Write it with the minor premise before the major premise.
?
Identify the minor and major premises in the following syllogism used in kalam: 'Every accident has a cause; the world is composed of accidents; therefore the world has a cause.' Then rearrange if needed.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Two Conditions for Syllogistic Soundness: Matter and Form
Instructions on distinguishing sound from unsound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate a syllogism's soundness by checking both the truth of its premises (material condition) and the validity of its form (formal condition).

Core Idea
A sound syllogism requires both true premises and a valid form.

Al-Bajuri explains that a sound premise must be sound in two respects: material and formal. This distinction is crucial for distinguishing valid arguments from fallacious ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Write out the syllogism clearly. For example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.'
The conclusion is usually preceded by 'therefore' or 'so'.
2
Check material truth of each premise
Examine whether each premise is factually true according to Islamic sources (Qur'an, Sunnah, consensus, or sound reason). A false premise violates the material condition.
For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is true based on hadith. 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' may be true if nabidh is defined as an intoxicating drink.
3
Check formal validity
Verify that the syllogism's form satisfies the conditions of validity: the middle term must be distributed, no illicit terms, etc. In a categorical syllogism, the form must correspond to a valid figure and mood.
The above example is in the first figure (Barbara) with valid form.
4
Determine soundness
If both conditions are met, the syllogism is sound. If either fails, it is unsound (fasid).
Al-Bajuri says the unsound includes both false premises (material) and invalid form.

The Syllogism

The Necessary Consequence Follows the Constancy of Truth
Author explains that the consequence follows the premises and the minor premise must fall under the major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between constant truth and accidental truth in syllogistic conclusions and explain how the constancy of premises affects the constancy of the conclusion.

Core Idea
A conclusion's constancy depends on the premises' constancy, not merely their truth.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies a subtle point: the necessary consequence (al-lāzim) follows the premises not in terms of truth or falsehood, but in terms of constancy of truth (iṭrād al-ṣidq). This distinction prevents a common misunderstanding that false premises always yield a false conclusion. We will explore this with an example and see how it applies to arguments in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author's Claim
Al-Bajuri states that the necessary consequence comes in accordance with the premises in terms of constancy of truth. Find the phrase 'بِحَسَبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ آتِ' and note that he interprets 'آتِ' as meaning coming in accordance with the premises in constancy.
2
Understand 'Iṭrād al-Ṣidq'
Iṭrād al-ṣidq means being constantly true (always true) rather than accidentally true. If the premises are constantly true, the conclusion is constantly true; if not, the conclusion is not constantly true. Accidental truth can occur even with false premises.
This refutes a potential objection: false premises do not necessitate false conclusion.
3
Analyze the Given Example
The example: 'Every human is inanimate; every inanimate is rational; therefore every human is rational.' Here premises are false, but the conclusion is accidentally true. The author explains that this does not contradict the rule because constancy is about the habitual truth, not occasional truth.
The syllogistic form is valid, but the truth of the conclusion is not constant because premises are not constantly true.
4
Apply to an Islamic Argument
Consider a Kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' If these premises are constantly true (known by necessary knowledge or revelation), the conclusion is constantly true. If any premise is only accidentally true, the conclusion's constancy is undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۸) فَإِنَّ لَازِمَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ آتِ (۷۹) وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُهَا فِي الكُبْرَى. قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنَّ لَازِمَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ ... إلخ) تَعْلِيلُ لِمَضْمُونِ البَيْتَيْنِ قَبْلَهُ. وَقَوْلُهُ : بِحَسَبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ آتِ أَيْ: آتٍ بِطَبْقِهَا وَوَفْقِهَا مِنْ حَيْثُ اطْرَادِ الصِّدْقِ وَعَدَمِ اطْرَادِهِ. فَإِنْ كَانَتِ المُقَدِّمَاتُ مُطَرِدَةَ الصِّدْقِ كَانَ لَازِمُهَا كَذَلِكَ، وَإِنْ لَمْ تَكُنْ مُطَرِدَةَ الصِّدْقِ كَانَ لَازِمُهَا كَذَلِكَ. وَبِتَقْرِيرِ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ عَلَى هَذَا الوَجْهِ انْدَفَعَ مَا قَدْ يُقَالُ: مُقْتَضَى كَلَامِهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ كَذِبُ لَازِمِهَا، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، بَلْ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ اللَّازِمُ مَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ جَمَادٌ، وَكُلُّ جَمَادٍ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِنَّ لَازِمَهُمَا وَهُوَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، صَادِقٌ. وَوَجْهُ الانْدِفَاعِ : أَنَّ المُرَادَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنِ اطْرادِهَا صِدْقاً أَطْرَادُهُ صِدْقاً، وَمِنْ عَدَمِ اطْرَادِهَا صِدْقاً عَدَمُ اطْرَادِهِ صِدْقاً، وَهَذَا لَا يُنَافِي أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ اتِّفَاقاً كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى.. إلخ) «مَا : اسْمُ مَوْصُولٍ بِمَعْنَى الَّتِي، وَصُغْرَى» : خَبَرٌ لِمُبْتَدَأَ مَحْذُوفٍ، وَالجُمْلَةُ صِلَةُ «ما»، وَمِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ : حَالٌ ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَالَّتِي هِيَ صُغْرَى حَالَ كَوْنِهَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ ... إلخ.
(78) For the necessary consequence of the premises comes. (79) And whichever of the premises is minor, its inclusion in the major is necessary. His statement: 'For the necessary consequence of the premises...' etc. is a justification for the content of the two preceding verses. And his statement: 'according to the premises comes' i.e., it comes in accordance with them in terms of the constancy of truth and its lack. If the premises are constantly true, then their consequence is likewise; if they are not constantly true, then their consequence is likewise. And by interpreting the author's statement in this manner, what might be objected is refuted: that his statement implies that falsehood of premises entails falsehood of their consequence, which is not the case; indeed, the consequence may be true despite false premises, as in your saying: 'Every human is inanimate, and every inanimate is rational,' for their consequence, which is 'Every human is rational,' is true. The refutation is that what is meant is: from their constant truth follows the constant truth of the consequence, and from the lack of their constant truth follows the lack of constant truth of the consequence. This does not contradict the possibility that it is true accidentally, as in the mentioned example. So consider it. His statement: 'And whichever of the premises is minor...' etc. 'Ma' is a relative pronoun meaning 'that which,' and 'minor' is the predicate of an omitted subject, and the sentence is the relative clause of 'ma,' and 'of the premises' is a circumstantial clause; the implied meaning is: 'and that which is minor while being of the premises...' etc.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage explains two verses (78 and 79) about the properties of premises in a syllogism. Verse 78 states that the necessary consequence (the conclusion) follows from the premises. The commentator clarifies that this does not mean that if the premises are false, the conclusion must be false; rather, it means that if the premises are consistently true, the conclusion is consistently true, and if they are not consistently true, the conclusion is not consistently true. Accidental truth of the conclusion despite false premises is possible, as in the example 'Every human is inanimate, every inanimate is rational' which yields the true conclusion 'Every human is rational.' Verse 79 states that whichever premise is the minor must be included (indirāj) in the major. The commentator provides a syntactic analysis of the sentence: 'ma' is a relative pronoun, 'minor' is a predicate of an omitted subject, and 'of the premises' is a circumstantial clause. This means that the minor premise, as one of the premises, must be subsumed under the major premise, typically because its subject is included in the subject of the major or in a way that the middle term connects them.

Key Terms
لازم
اطراد الصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, if the premises are not constantly true, what can we say about the necessary consequence (conclusion)?
A) The conclusion must be false.
B) The conclusion is not constantly true, though it may be accidentally true.
C) The conclusion is constantly true regardless of the premises.
D) The conclusion cannot be known.

Key Takeaway
The constancy of truth in the conclusion follows the constancy of truth in the premises; accidental truth does not guarantee constancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is haram.' Are the premises constantly true? Is the conclusion constantly true? Explain your reasoning.
?
Construct a syllogism from Fiqh where the premises are not constantly true (i.e., they are based on a weak evidence) and discuss whether the conclusion can be considered certain.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Minor Premise Must Be Included in the Major
Author explains that the consequence follows the premises and the minor premise must fall under the major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify that a minor premise is properly included under the major premise in a categorical syllogism and explain why this is necessary for validity.

Core Idea
The minor premise must be included under the major for the syllogism to be sound in form.

Al-Bajuri next explains that the minor premise must be included (indirāj) in the major premise. This is a technical condition for a valid categorical syllogism: the subject of the minor must be a subset of the subject of the major, which connects them via the middle term. Let's unpack this.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Claim
Find the phrase 'وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُهَا فِي الكُبْرَى'. This states that whichever premise is minor, its inclusion in the major is necessary. The author provides a syntactic analysis indicating that 'minor' is a predicate of an omitted subject.
2
Understand 'Indirāj'
Indirāj means inclusion or subsumption. In a first-figure categorical syllogism, the minor premise asserts that its subject (S) is included in the subject of the major premise (M). For example, in 'All S are M; all M are P; therefore all S are P', the minor 'All S are M' shows that S is a subset of M.
This condition ensures the syllogism is in the first figure and valid.
3
Test with a Fiqh Example
Consider: 'Every intoxicant is haram' (major), 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor). Here, the minor premise states that nabidh (S) is included in intoxicant (M). This inclusion is true, so the syllogism is valid in form. The conclusion 'Nabidh is haram' follows necessarily.
If the minor were 'Nabidh is a beverage', that would not be included in 'intoxicant', so the syllogism would be invalid.

(۷۸) فَإِنَّ لَازِمَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ آتِ (۷۹) وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُهَا فِي الكُبْرَى. قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنَّ لَازِمَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ ... إلخ) تَعْلِيلُ لِمَضْمُونِ البَيْتَيْنِ قَبْلَهُ. وَقَوْلُهُ : بِحَسَبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ آتِ أَيْ: آتٍ بِطَبْقِهَا وَوَفْقِهَا مِنْ حَيْثُ اطْرَادِ الصِّدْقِ وَعَدَمِ اطْرَادِهِ. فَإِنْ كَانَتِ المُقَدِّمَاتُ مُطَرِدَةَ الصِّدْقِ كَانَ لَازِمُهَا كَذَلِكَ، وَإِنْ لَمْ تَكُنْ مُطَرِدَةَ الصِّدْقِ كَانَ لَازِمُهَا كَذَلِكَ. وَبِتَقْرِيرِ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ عَلَى هَذَا الوَجْهِ انْدَفَعَ مَا قَدْ يُقَالُ: مُقْتَضَى كَلَامِهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ كَذِبُ لَازِمِهَا، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، بَلْ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ اللَّازِمُ مَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ جَمَادٌ، وَكُلُّ جَمَادٍ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِنَّ لَازِمَهُمَا وَهُوَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، صَادِقٌ. وَوَجْهُ الانْدِفَاعِ : أَنَّ المُرَادَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنِ اطْرادِهَا صِدْقاً أَطْرَادُهُ صِدْقاً، وَمِنْ عَدَمِ اطْرَادِهَا صِدْقاً عَدَمُ اطْرَادِهِ صِدْقاً، وَهَذَا لَا يُنَافِي أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ اتِّفَاقاً كَمَا فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى.. إلخ) «مَا : اسْمُ مَوْصُولٍ بِمَعْنَى الَّتِي، وَصُغْرَى» : خَبَرٌ لِمُبْتَدَأَ مَحْذُوفٍ، وَالجُمْلَةُ صِلَةُ «ما»، وَمِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ : حَالٌ ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَالَّتِي هِيَ صُغْرَى حَالَ كَوْنِهَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ ... إلخ.
(78) For the necessary consequence of the premises comes. (79) And whichever of the premises is minor, its inclusion in the major is necessary. His statement: 'For the necessary consequence of the premises...' etc. is a justification for the content of the two preceding verses. And his statement: 'according to the premises comes' i.e., it comes in accordance with them in terms of the constancy of truth and its lack. If the premises are constantly true, then their consequence is likewise; if they are not constantly true, then their consequence is likewise. And by interpreting the author's statement in this manner, what might be objected is refuted: that his statement implies that falsehood of premises entails falsehood of their consequence, which is not the case; indeed, the consequence may be true despite false premises, as in your saying: 'Every human is inanimate, and every inanimate is rational,' for their consequence, which is 'Every human is rational,' is true. The refutation is that what is meant is: from their constant truth follows the constant truth of the consequence, and from the lack of their constant truth follows the lack of constant truth of the consequence. This does not contradict the possibility that it is true accidentally, as in the mentioned example. So consider it. His statement: 'And whichever of the premises is minor...' etc. 'Ma' is a relative pronoun meaning 'that which,' and 'minor' is the predicate of an omitted subject, and the sentence is the relative clause of 'ma,' and 'of the premises' is a circumstantial clause; the implied meaning is: 'and that which is minor while being of the premises...' etc.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage explains two verses (78 and 79) about the properties of premises in a syllogism. Verse 78 states that the necessary consequence (the conclusion) follows from the premises. The commentator clarifies that this does not mean that if the premises are false, the conclusion must be false; rather, it means that if the premises are consistently true, the conclusion is consistently true, and if they are not consistently true, the conclusion is not consistently true. Accidental truth of the conclusion despite false premises is possible, as in the example 'Every human is inanimate, every inanimate is rational' which yields the true conclusion 'Every human is rational.' Verse 79 states that whichever premise is the minor must be included (indirāj) in the major. The commentator provides a syntactic analysis of the sentence: 'ma' is a relative pronoun, 'minor' is a predicate of an omitted subject, and 'of the premises' is a circumstantial clause. This means that the minor premise, as one of the premises, must be subsumed under the major premise, typically because its subject is included in the subject of the major or in a way that the middle term connects them.

Key Terms
اند راج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what is required of the minor premise in relation to the major premise?
A) It must be true.
B) It must be included in the major premise.
C) It must be the same as the major premise.
D) It must be false.

Key Takeaway
The minor premise must be included under the major for the syllogism to be valid; this is the condition of indirāj.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the major premise 'Every action that causes harm is prohibited' and the minor premise 'Smoking causes harm', check whether the minor is properly included under the major. Formulate the conclusion.
?
Identify the inclusion relationship in the syllogism: 'A woman's testimony is half that of a man's in financial transactions; Zaynab's testimony is a woman's testimony; therefore Zaynab's testimony is half that of a man's in financial transactions.' Is the inclusion valid? Why?
